“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia's Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper iqhich has a consistent an! dii
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians. —The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System- one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole_
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility.”
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
asu has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin's inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily papa-
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they arc printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushraan of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-slieller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Subscription (payable strictly in advance), Six
Shillings and Sixpence per Quarter, or Otic Pound
per Annum.
Businas communications to he addressed to
William Macleod, Managing Director, to whom
all remittances arc payable. Editorial communi-
cations to be addressed to J. F. Archibald.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty Shillings
sterling—which you may pay by Post Office Order, One
Pound Note, or Stamps of any Australian colony—you
can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week
for a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.

SATURDAY, JULY 9, 1892.
The British Elections.
The British Conservative Parliament has
died of old age and chronic uselessness, and
the two ancient parties —Avhich never did
any particular good save to keep each other
out of office, and which never did any
particular harm save that Avhich is embodied
in the fact that they never are both out at
the same time—have arisen to save their
bleeding country once more. Also, three
subordinate parties—making five in all—arc
pervading the land, and great is the con-
fusion thereof. In the first place, there is
the venerable Conservative faction the
stupid faction Avith the huge stomach—The
great hereditary landlords avlio support the
Constitution, Avhatever that may be, and
tlie aristocracy, and the Roj 7 al Family, and
the Coercion laws in Ireland, and the State
Church, and avlio never did any single act
in the Avay of progress except Avhea they
Avere'dragged into it by the relentless force
of circumstances. Attached to them is the
Unionist section Avhich calls itself Liberal
and votes Avith the Conservatives—a poor
little mongrel organisation of no particular
account, headed by a renegade screw-maker
and a duke, and obviously doomed to extinc-
tion. These two parties represent the
element Avhich wants to do nothing except
to maintain the Throne and collect its
rents regularly, and Avhich fancies that the
Avorld can be made to stand still provided
the House of Commons passes a Bill
to that effect. Third on the list is
the old Liberal party Avhich, like the
Conservative one, never progresses unless it
is dragged along by exterior influences, and
Avhich is called Liberal merely because it
doesn’t require to be dragged quite so hard
as its rival. After this comes the Irish
Parnellite party, which Avants freedom for
Ireland, but is prepared to sacrifice it, if
necessary, because of its reverence for a
dead man’s tombstone ; and, last of all, there
is the Irish M‘Carthyite party, Avhich also
Avants freedom for Ireland, but is also pre-
pared to sacrifice it, if needful, because of
its want of reverence for the tombstone afore-
said. And, as for the party which Avishes to
prevent tlie accumulation of huge fortunes
and the consequent impoverishment of the
masses—which desires to tax the big land-
lords, and burst up the big estates, and
shorten the hours of tlie overburdened
worker, by Act of Parliament if necessary,
and to wipe out sweating, and stop the
ruinous influx of foreign paupers and foreign
shoddy, it has not yet been heard of at all
in British politics.
* * *
Tlie old snnfilebusters of Britain have
always thrown in their lot with the Tory
faction, while the new snuflflebusters will
probably give a tolerably unanimous support
to the Liberals. The former comprise the
Established Church, which collects its tithes
from the beggared farmers by moans of
bailiffs and police, and seizes the hist pig
from the ruined agriculturist in the name
of the great Reformer of Nazareth.
The latter include all the Nonconformist
bodies, and their apostle is Stead and their
ablest political organ the Review of
Reviews. The Old Snuffle revolves around
Queen Victoria, and its great axiom is that
she rules by Divine right, and has been en-
dowed by Providence with more than human
virtues ; the New Snuffle has no such well-
defined centre, but exhibits a marked
tendency to whitewash the Prince of Wales.
Both of them preach that Discontent is
sinful above all things, and that the famished
and rag-chid millions who sleep on broken
floors and under leaky roofs, and live in the
odour of sewer-gas, and are ignorant, and
frozen, and unspeakably poor, and without
hope either in this world or the next, should
be calm and resigned and should put their
trust in the Lord. They both denounce
Socialism as a wicked flying in the face of
Providence, and revolution as a thing that is
born of Anti-CnniST, and their remedy for
the indescribable misery of outcast England
is always prayer, which is never answered,
and faith, which is the art of believing in
things which we know are not and cannot
be true. Conjointly they represent that
enormously powerful influence, the pious
drivel of England—a multitudinous, black-
coated, black-hatted incubus —a vast mass of
“respectable’’greasy, shop-keeping humanity
which is impervious to sorrow at home, but
is always ready to shed copious tears over a
dead ass in Central Africa, ora happy nigger
who hasn’t been blessed with rum and
Scripture on some coral island among the
South Seas.
* # #
The politicians of England, like its
churches, are stereotyped and petrified, and,
so far as concerns the great social problem of
chronic poverty and woe, there is no hope in
them. Gladstone is, at the present time,
tlie most advanced by far among the leading
statesmen of the country, but except with
regard to the one question of Irish autonomy,
he is slow and bigoted and incurably Con-
servative. In Australia he would be regarded
as a left-off curiosity of the dark ages of
politics, and when England calls him an
enlightened Liberal it is a woeful commen-
tary on English Liberalism, He is rigidly
opposed to the compulsory eight - hours
system, because it would bo an interference
with “individual liberty”—which means
the liberty of the employer to work his em-
ploye sixteen hours a day if he wants to, and
the glorious freedom of the employe to
accept these terms if he feels so inclined or
else to starve. He is equally opposed to the
enforcement by law of a ten or twelve or a
fourteen hours’ system for exactly the same
reasons—they are all an interference with
individual liberty. Even a sixteen-hour
system, according to the Gladstonian philo-
sophy, is injurious to freedom, for the
employers who want to exact seventeen
hours work—and they are not a few— would
thereby be subjected to a bloated tyranny,
and Socialism would begin to make inroads
upon that indefinite thing called the Constitu-
tion. The idea of imposingaland-taxandcom-
pelling the British landholder to bear even the
same proportion of the national burdens as
any other man is too utterly and horribly
sacrilegious ever to have dawned upon
Gladstone’s intelligence. Ho is an ardent
supporter of the Established Church which
exacts £8,000,000 annually from the hungry
nation by force and fraud and snuffle, and
legal violence. If any one were to suggest
that England should shut its gates against the
ceaseless inroad of foreign paupers who are
steadily reducing wages to starvation level,
or propose the adoption of tlie French law
of inheritance so that the huge estates and
huge fortunes which overshadow the country
might be gradually dissipated among the
mass of the people, he would probably civil
out the troops under the impression that a
revolution had begun. And even a proposal
to stop the destruction of agriculture and
the eviction of the agricultural population in
order to create deer-forests and grouse moors
would make his aged hair and his huge shirt-
collar stand upon end with horror.
* # *
The one gleam of Liberalism in all Glad-
stone’s policy is his desire to grant
autonomy to Ireland, and so far as is known
his scheme is so hedged in with petty
restrictions and reservations as to be little
better than a shadow. And beyond that he
is simply an advocate of tlie horrors and the
crimes of liberty—the liberty ot every man
to do wrong if he has the power, and to seize
his neighbour's substance if he finds tlie
chance, and to grind the faces of the poor
whenever the opportunity presents itself.
And one-half the British Parliament follows
him cautiously and reluctantly, as an able
but revolutionary old man who is worthy of
support but must not be supported too reck-
lessly ; while the other half regards him as a
wild, mad, unscrupulous iconoclast who
ought to be abolished, lest he should drag
England into anarchy and ruin. There is
not in all Gladstone’s policy one gleam of
genuine sympathy with the starving, hope-
less, wretched, sorrow - stricken masses of
England. Be is merely an able and eloquent
political machine, a deeply sincere snufiler
over forms and ceremonies and creeds, one
more among that great crowd who urge the
poor and the homeless to trust in Providence
—and be “loft.” When Stead on a recent
occasion asked him what he considered to bo
the greatest hope for the future in England
his answer was worthy of the British poli-
tician—such an answer as is eminently calcu-
lated to bring comfort to a sorrowing
nation
I should say ive must look for that to the maintenance
of faith in the Invisible. That is the great hope of tlie
future ; it is ttic mainstay of civilisation. And lay that I
mean a living faith in n personal God. Ido not hold with
“ streams of tendency.” After CO years of public life I
hold more strongly than over to this comiction, deepened
and strengthened by long experience, of the reality, and
the nearness, and the personality of God.
Yet Gladstone, despite his bigotry, his
Conservatism, and his shufflings, is by far
the most advanced among the leading states-
men of England. He is all but useless to
the cause of progress, yet he is better than
his compeers because they are useless alto-
gether.
ss * »
The attempt to form a powerful fifth party
in the interests of Labour is for the time fore-
doomed to failure. Only men of independent
means can sit in the British Parliament
under the present system ; the country is
owned by the caucuses of the two leading
parties, and for the candidate who is not
nominated by cither of them there is hardly
one chance in a hundred ; the workmen who
attempt to organise a party of their own are
liable to be boycotted and starved by the
organisations of capital; and even though
the House of Commons could be conquered
by the Labour-party the Lords are still in
the way and can be shifted only by terrorism
or revolution. And—the worst disability of
all—both the Old Snuffle and the-New are
against the Labour organisations, on the
theory that the masses should stick to the
old parties and trust entirely to the Lord
for deliverance from their own personal
troubles. And betwixt the twin powers of
snuffle and the caucuses the chances of the
people arc not bright. The coming struggle
lies between two dead men, and it is only a
question which corpse will win. The fight
is between the dead Parnell as representing
Irish autonomy, and the dead William of
Orange as the emblem of Irish subjection,
and tlie probabilities arc in favour of the
former as being the fresher dead man of the
two, and one who is worth more dead than
the other was when alive. But there is
nothing in the programme of the one
deceased or the other which promises
to relieve, in the slightest degree, the suffer-
ings of a nation which is oppressed by the
twofold evils of landlordism and capitalism.
* # *
Still, dark as the horizon may be, it is
brighter than it has ever been before. The
Labour-party in the new Parliament will be
exceedingly small beyond a doubt, yet
the chances arc that the nucleus of a party
will be formed. Tlie Liberal Unionists are,
to all appearance, dead, and the shattered
fragment that remains after the election will
probably unite with the Tories and have a
separate existence no longer. Home Rule
will bo accomplished sooner or later, and
when it is accomplished the Kilkenny cats
who are now howling over the great
Parnell’s dust will remove to College
Green, and the House of Commons will
fall back into something approaching its
ancient position. Then the Labour members
may form the Third Party in the House,
and if they can only find a leader half
as able, dauntless and enduring as the
noble Irish patriot who was howled to
death by the prurient apostles of the New
Snuffle in England, they can make the House
of Commons a hell as ho did, until some
future Premier undergoes another sudden
conversion even as Gladstone did on
the subject of Ireland. And after they
have been jeered by the dreary comic
prints, and gaoled for sedition and attempted
revolution, and howled down by the high-
born “gentlemen” of the Commons, and
cursed, and excommunicated, and suspended,
and expelled, then some Liberal leader of
the future will adopt their programme under
compulsion, and stump the country with it
until he achieves a triumph. For in England
everything comes to him who waits and
remorselessly stonewalls the Legislature from
one year’s end to the other, and oven the
mighty influence of the national snuffle gives
way to him in the end.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Camel Problem.
“ Unionist ” Avrites to The Bulletin as follows
from Bourke (N.S.W.) re an alleged camol-
shooting case in that district :
As the Camel-question is one of very great importance
to the inhabitants in this district, and as Tim Bulletin
—(&c., &c. —marmalade omitted. —Ed. B.) —I sei d yon tlie
enclosed report of a police-court case, being of the opinion
that yon would he able to make use of part of it. Know-
ing tiiat your sympathies are with the workers, and as the
introduction of camels and Afghans is worse than the
introduction of Chinese to the masses, especially in this
district, I enclose the cutting for you to make use of or
otherwise.
There is a hopeless conservatism about this
effusion that is depressing to contemplate. The
Australian bush man is seemingly so wedded to
his horse and his bullock-dray that he is re-
solved to have no improvement, and—presum-
ably in his interests—a bill was even introduced
into Parliament last session to restrict the use
of the only animal that is capable of doing good
service on the hungry, sun-stricken, waterless
plains of the interior. There is no earthly reason
why the Afghan and the camel should go together;
the Australian has at least as much intelligence
as that imported Asiatic, and he knows enough
to make use of the “ship of the desert ” with-
out hiring any cheap Mohammedan to help him.
Put, apparently, he never dreams of making
the attempt, and because the Afghan is another
cheap labour curse in a land where such curses
are already much too plentiful, therefore he
wants to abolish him and his animal together.
The idea of abolishing the man and not the
animal has not yet, so far as we are aware,
been proposed by anybody. Unless Providence
will send rivers through all the dry lands of
Australia, or endow the horse with the capacity
of travelling for four days under a burning sun
without water, and carrying 4001bs. on its back,
and living, when necessary, upon anything that
bears the slightest semblance to vegetation, and
can make that animal live for 40 years, the
camel has come to stay in this country, and of
the two animals the horse could, in the waterless
interior, be better spared. If the Australian is
too conservative to learn the use of the humped
monstrosity, then the Afghan driver will stay ;
otherwise he can go. The individual who wants
to abolisli camels because the first of their kind
was driven by cheap Asiatics is capable of
abolishing the bullock to get rid of some occa-
sional Chinese bullock-driver, or tearing up the
railways in order to secure the abolition of
imported railway commissioners.

Tlie “ National Ass ’’—Again!
The bogus-National Association of New South
Wales last week sent out this private circular
among the Sydney amateur athletes and mem-
bers of athletic societies :
Dear Sir,-Owing to indifference, or for some oilier
reason, the younger men of this colony have not hitherto
taken that part in its public affairs which was warranted
by their numbers and influence,; and it has been thought
well to invite the representatives of the various rowing,
football, cricket, cycling, and kindred bodies to meet
together informally for the purpose of talking over the
position of affairs, with a view to obtaining the co-opera-
tion of the members of their clubs in all matters relating
to the public welfare. A preliminary smoke-meeting,
which you are cordially invited to attend, will be held on
Saturday evening next, July 2 (at 8 o’clock, in the front
room upstairs at the Sydney Catering Co.’s premises
(Gunsler’s), 175 Pitt-sireet), and it is hoped, that you will
make it convenient to attend and secure the attendance
of any of youi friends who are likely to he interested.
Yours, faithfully,
(Signed)
Most. Arnold, A. Consett Stephen, T. 11. Fairland,
A. C. Ebswoutii, E. P. Simpson, William Cope, 11. Y.
Braddox, Adrian Knox, W. 11. German, George Merri
vale.
William Epps, Secretary.
The meeting duly came off and was attended
by about 20 people. The irrepressible Mr.
“Billy” Cope was elected to the chair, and
had carefully taken off his braces and left his
waistcoat unbuttoned to give a free-and-easy
tone to things A section of the meeting were
Randwick footballers who bad just achieved an
unexpected victory over Strathfield, and
they were “ ge-lorious.” They assiduously
punished the liquor which the National
Ass was fool enough to provide, applauded every-
body, and drank success to everything.
The notion of mixing up football and Freetrade,
pedestrianism and Protection, is one which
would only occur to an inspired idiot, but the
National Ass has a purpose to serve, and hopes
to use the footballers, &c., as tools to effect
that purpose. It imagines that if it softsawders
the athletic bodies they will roll up at Free-
trade meetings and act as cheap chuckers-out
(ft la the pugs, at the celebrated packed Sydney
Jubilee-gathering) and do the dirty work for
the Association generally. “We want your
assistance,” whined Secretary Epps to the
meeting the oilier night. “ For what object
do you want our assistance?” lie was asked,
but no satisfactory reply was forthcoming. The
promoters were frightened to come right out
with the truth and say what they wanted.
They pretended that they were actuated solely
by a desire to. awaken interest in politics, and
they had the effrontery to state that their asso-
ciation did not take any political side. The
platform of the association is to oppose “ One-
man-one-vote,” and if that is not taking a side
in politics, then there is no such thing as taking
a side. Everyone knows well enough that the
National Ass is a capitalistic association
—the names on its circulars prove it.
Can the footballers and others believe that the
men belonging to the National Association are
spending money, and hiring rooms, and sending
out circulars, and paying for drinks, simply
from a philanthropic desire to see young men
vote? Do the names of the National Associa-
tion look like that class of men? “No fear.”
The thing is simply a sickly attempt to run in
the athletic bodies to act as “ barrackers ” for
the white waistcoat party.
*. .
An Impending Uproar.
A shriek of indignation will presently come
over the sea from London, when the “home”
shareholders who have met the calls made by
certain Melbourne banks (in liquidation) learn
how their Australian brethren skip over the
difficulty. One after another the damaged
capitalists of Marvellous M. are dodging their
liabilities in the calm, respectable manner
peculiar to land-boom morality. Instead of
wearing their schedules on the sleeve as an
outward visible sign that they have “ taken
the knock,” these people seem to go bung on an
inward spiritual system, whereby they are
enabled to keep a veneer of dignity unspotted
before the world, whilst they get there all the
Same. The head and front of a big commercial
firm is said to have opened a private meeting of
his own particular creditors with prayer, pre-
vious to arranging a small composition, which
will entitle him to escape from his calls with a
loss of so-muc',i in the £. That a recently
almighty financier has paid 5 per cent, on h s
most pressing liabilities, and gone silently away
in the track of Matthew Davies, is pretty
generally understo xl. The fact that a high-toned


speculator squared his circle with a half-dollar
dividend seems to be common property. Mis-
fortune falls very lightly upon the representa-
tive city “ gent.” or influential bubble-ldower in
this part of the world. The struggling trades-
man who, considering that when luck goes
against him he must take the consequences,
sells himself up and divides all his goods
amongst the poor creditors, has to stand aside
in C'ollins-street and let the “ friendly composi-
tion ” potentate go by, for the latter takes the
middle of the pavement as boldly as ever and
treads down the humble citizens in his path.
This state of things may meet ■ with local
approval, but when it ceases to be a matter of
purely Australian interest the trouble is bound
to come. If a Londoner and a Melbournite are
supposed to be pulling in the same losing boat,
a nd the cockney who plys his sculls finds that
his comrade has quietly slunk out of the job
and swum to shore—well, the affair must surely
eventuate in a row.
———
The Broken Hill Strike.
This week there has been signed what—Jo all
appearance —is the death-warrant of the great
Broken Hill mining corporation. For many
weeks past there have been rumours of impend-
ing trouble at Broken Hill. The huge mine
has, judging by results, always been worked on
“the pig-rooting” principle, with the one
object of getting out the richest ore first,
and getting it out anyhow, at the risk of
the lives of everybody concerned save the
board of directors. There has been a horrible
lack of engineering skill in the management of
things, and the workings are perpetually on the
verge of collapse, and the workers underground
dig out the company’s dividends at constant
risk of entombment. These dividends have been
enormous ; the Broken Hill Proprietary Co. re-
Eays its original capital regularly every six weeks,
ut the shares have been artificially “boosted” on
the stock exchange to such a height that the
buyer at present prices receives a very small
percentage upon his outlay. Therefore, the
capitalistic press points to this small percentage
as a proof that the mine is ruined by the
exorbitant demands of the employes, and that
wages must be reduced in order that the suffer-
ing proprietors may receive a reasonable return
for their money. There is a general feeling
that 860 per cent, profit is insufficient, and a
longing to enhance these returns by any possible
means. The Broken Hill Proprietary. Co.
has got what is practically a perpetual lease of
the richest ore deposits in Australia at an
infinitesimal rent. It pays larger salaries to
its directors and other high officials than any
other company of its kind in this continent,'
and it pays the lowest wages to its workmen.
Rents are high at Broken Hill, the cost of
living is enormous, the town is woefully
unhealthy, and the miners suffer much from
that horrible complaint known as lead-poison-
ing, and in return for all these disabilities their
pay has been reduced far below the accustomed
Australian standard. And in addition to all
these troubles the Proprietary,Co.—not satisfied
with its profits of £1,000,000 per annum or
thereabouts —recently gave notice that it in-
tended to break its agreement with the men—
the agreement that was solemnly ratified
by both parties in 1890 —and run the
mine by the contract, free competition
system, letting the work out in instal-
ments to the cheapest sweater who might
come along and leaving him to make his profits
in the good old way of the sweating system in
general. The miners I'equested a conference
upon the subject, and being unable to obtain a
definite answer they decided to refuse the terms
and leave off work, and now if the mine wants
to cave in, it must cave. On this subject the
Sydney Daily Telegraph —that hypocritical
organ which is always ready to advocate Land
Nationalisation, or the millennium, or any far-
off reform which it believes will never be
realised, and which always denounces any
reform that seems in danger of coming to pass
—states the case in the interests of Property as
follows
For some time past the state of the Proprietary mine,
in which the bulk of the men are employed, has been
such as to occasion grave fears for its safety'. The timbers
have been subjected to a tremendous strain, and it is only
by the most strenuous exertions that a ruinous collapse
has been so far prevented. The danger of this disaster is
regarded as still serious, and unless the work of
strengthening the supports is pushed on property to the
extent of millions may be destroyed in as many moments.
This difficulty is ccn ileredto be Labour’s opportunity,
n the men were to continue at work until the term of
notice has expired the mine would be secure, but by

Presently a foreigner offers to let B and C have

acceptance is made. Then what follows?
Either A is thrown out of employment, and
being unable to pay for his maintenance becomes
a direct burden on B and C, who reluctantly
are compelled to support him, or A supplies B
and C with boots at 7s. lid. a pair, and owing
to his diminished income is forthwith compelled
to reduce his consumption of B and C’s goods
unless these latter are also agreeable to accept a
reduction in their costs of production and
charge only 7s, lid. each for their goods like-
wise. The indirect advantages of Protection
are a bit above the feeble Freetrade intellect.
Cable item from Britain :
At the Chambers of Commerce Congress this afternoon
Mr. Nealey moved the resolution standing in his name,
recommending the self-governing colonies of the British
Empire to adopt a policy of freetrade. In speaking to
the question he declared that the continuance of protec-
tion would have the effect of destroyin': the commercial
treaties between Great Britain and Belgium and Germany.
Foreign nations would adopt measures of retaliation in
respect to colonial raw materials. It was quite possible
to make the ports of all the colonies duty free to foreign
goods, but it was a matter of impossibility to convert
England to a protectionist country.
The calm self-sufficiency of the blatant Briton
never shone out more conspicuously than in
that little item. It would be quite possible to
make “all the colonies” freetrade because
it would be—haw—convenient to Britain
that they should be so, and Nealey
evidently feels that when these “colonies”
hear that Britain’s commercial treaties
are in danger they will fall over each other in
their efforts to sacrifice themselves for her
benefit. And Nealey would probably be an
astonished Briton if he were told that the
“colonies” are at present engaged in looking
after their own interests, and they didn’t even
know that Britain had commercial treaties with
Belgium and Germany ; and if these treaties are
so fixed that they require Australia to go back
to Freetrade, then they had better be refixed
on a new basis forthwith. And as regards
Nealey’s recommendation that “ the self-
governing colonies adopt a policy of Freetrade ”
the said “colonies ” hereby return the kindness
by recommending Britain to adopt Protection
and to abolish the Queen and the House of
Lords, for if she doesn’t she may seriously
injure our relations with Honolulu or some
place, and then this country will feel aggra-
vated.
In the Days of the Lash and the
Darbies.
To uplift the honour, glory, and credit of the
mother-province, the N.S. W. Government is now
actively engaged in carrying on the patriotic work
of holding up the mirror to convict nature —a
work initiated some years ago by The Bulletin
in its “ History of Botany Bay,” and subsequently
continued in “Tales of the System.” 'To give
vraisemblanee to a consistently lurid narrative, to
throw also into high relief his own actions in regard
to convietism, and to deepen the artistic gloom
from which he claims to have rescued this God-
benisoned territory, Sir Henry Farkes has taken
keen and artistic interest in the publication of
the “ History of New South Wales from the
Records,” with all the shadows thrown on such
history by the historic hulks and the primeval
gallows ; and now we have the records themselves
—and very edifying reading they make, indeed.
Some years ago the first volume of the “His-
tory of New South Wales” was issued to an
expectant public ; and all things considered it
fell little short of the expectations formed of it.
Noiv we have from Mr. Alex. Bntton the “Records”
upon which Mr. Barton’s volume was founded.
It is called part 2of volume I. The volume
in question, or part of a volume, as it pleases the
authorities to term it, is the Exodus of convietism
with a small section of its Deuteronomy. It is
luridly interesting. Here we have the skeleton,
the iilans and specifications of the gibbet ;
the abstract ethics of the “ system,” in all
their naked, brutal and unadulterated simplicity.
Here are the short and simple annals of the six
marines hanged for a paltry theft of provisions in
days of cruel privation ; we are naively told
that “Seven of them were concerned, but one of
them turned King’s evidence and saved his neck —
he was the greatest villain and first projector of
the scheme.” In 1790 a “ woman ” was judicially
strangled at Norfolk Island for robbery ; and
there are other hangings recorded in the book,
which need not here be chronicled. However,
hanging was too lovely and idyllic a death to
please the gentle Phillip (to whom, by the
orders of Sir Henry Farkes, an expensive
statue is being erected out of the revenue of
the province). He considered that death itself
was not enough, and that the criminal should be
confined “ till an opportunity offered of delivering
him as a prisoner to the natives of Maoriland —
and let them eat him.
* * *
The mortality-bills are interesting. Thirty-six
men and four women prisoners died on the passage
out of the First Fleet. Up to the 9th of July of
the same year (1788) 20 men and 8 women had
shuffled off their convict coil ; 11 men and 1
woman had absconded, 4 bad been executed, and
3 had been killed by the natives. Up to the end
of September Collins registers the death-rate as
100, exclusive of abscondmeuts and executions.
During the passage out the Lady Juliana lost only
7 women ; but the Second Fleet was the floating
altar of a perfect holocaust. “Of the 930 males
sent out by the last ship,” writes Governor Phillip,
“ 201 died on board, and 50 have died since land-
ing. The number of sick this day (17th June,
1790) is 45(Q and many who are not reckoned as
sick have barely strength to attend to themselves.
Such is our present state, and when the last ships
arrived we had not 00 people sick in the colony.”
King writes from the Cape of Good Hope in
July, 1792, and registers the deaths on board the
Active and three or four other transports as 39.
On board the Pitt 49 persons died of fever and
scurvy, and 120 of the convicts were sick when
landed. In March 1792, Phillip wrote ; “ I am
very sorry to say that most of the convicts who
were received by the last ships still continue in
tiie same debilitated state in which they were
landed, and of whom, in less than seven months,
228 men have died. In the seven months prior
to the arrival of those ships,' the deaths were 19.
The returns of sick this day ts—civil and military,
IS; male convicts, 394: and females, 17. ’ The
Royal Admiral, which landed a cargo of cjii-
*“ Historical jßecords of New South Wales” : Vol. 1.,
Part 2 Phillip; 1783-1792. By authority, Sidney ;
Charles Potter, Government Printer, 1892. Registered
under the Copy ri,dit Act, 1579,
victs on the 7th of October, 1702, had a terrible
outward voyage : Phillip writes to Secretary
Dundas—“Of the convicts embarked on board
that ship, 10 men and 2 women died on the
passage, and 4 children were born, one of whom
died ; one male convict escaped at the Cape of
Good Hope, and seventy-two men, eleven women,
and fire children have been landed sick.” Even
such a martinet as Phillip is moved to remark :
“I think the people have been too much crowded on
hoard this ship,”
# * *
A letter from Major Hess to Governor Phillip,
dated from Norfolk Island, reads quaintly enough.
The gallant Lieutenant-Governor growls irritably
because “confinement, with corporal punishment,
was all ” he was authorised to inflict as a punish-
ment for common “and garden” theft, as the
wicked and abandoned wretches plundered the vege-
table patches of the settlement almost without fear
when they knew they could not then be hanged
for it. King subsequently complains of his incom-
peteney to inflict severe punishments, and there
seems to have been an unspeakable yearning in
the official mind of the day to put human backs
and human necks (when belonging to convicts) to
the only useful purpose that God ordained them
for, viz., to be scored and scarred over with the
cat-o’-nine-tails in the first instance and to be
stretched by hemp in thfe second. There is a vast
amount of information in the present volume con-
cerning convicts, rations, discipline, punishment,
executions, leg-irons, gaol gangs and town
gangs, absconders, floggings, hangings, mutinies,
mortality on convict transports, shortage
of provisions, criminal mortality, condition
of the hulks, quarrels between the Governor
and the military, court-martials, convict-bar-
racks, the N.S. Wales Corps, outlawry,
thefts of boats, drownings, wrecks, floating
prisons, overcrowded transports, details respect-
ing the three Elects, and all sorts of transporta-
tion and convict statistics and penitentiary
theories, military establishments, derelictions of
marines, pirates, inland discoveries, and what-not.
The collection begins August, 1783, and ends
December, 1792, when Governor Phillip left Lag-
land for England, its mother. The whole period
covered is nine years and four months, during the
first three years of which proposals and sugges-
tions of convict colonisation were under considera-
tion, eight months were occupied in making pre-
parations for the departure of the First Fleet of
transports ; the voyage took up another eight
months ; and the remainder of the time, rather
more than five years, was devoted to hanging,
flogging, starving, and otherwise “ disciplining ”
the outcast mob of pickpockets and rabbit-
snarers and other small sinners of the dear old
mother-country. Those who outlived the rigours
of this period of probation founded the first
families of New South Wales, and their descen-
dants now run the province, so far as they
are able, on the lines laid down by
their great-grandfathers’ oppressors. The docu-
ments cover much ground. Matra, Young
and Banks are each represented in their separate
and special proposals relating to convict settle-
ment. Bligh receives a deal of notice in connec-
tion with the Bounty mutiny. The exploration of
the province in the very first days of settlement,
the voyaging of the Government craft about the
coasts, the squabbles between the officials, the
lies the different heads of departments told about
each other and the lies outsiders told about both
-have all here fair and unbiassed representation.
The volume is too vasty for exhaustive notice, but
it is unique, entertaining and provocative of high-
class cynicism. It throws an eager search-light
into the revered gloom of a historic period ; and
it is well worth the price of publication, whatever
that is. It is well bound, well-printed, admirably
“read,” and it reflects great credit upon all con-
cerned in its production. It has one glaring fault,
however. The index is not a triumph of any kind
whatever, and the value of a historical work of
such multifariousness as the one in question is
seriously discounted unless anyone interested can
readily and immediately discover what he is
looking for. With this hint for the conduct of
future volumes of “ Historical Kecords of New-
South Wales” we take our leave of the latest
collection of Convict Memoirs issued by the press.

The man, Cream, accused of poisoning
girls in London with a view to getting “hush-

that effect, already looks like a “goner.” On the

All the old Melbourne identities are
quitting their billets. Captain Eullarton, a
potentate of 30 years’ standing in the Vie. Naval
Brigade service, took a formal leave of his gallant
seamen one night last week. The capting ex-
pressed a hope that they would continue to scare
away the foe as vigorously as ever, and the heroes
shouted “ay, ay, Sir !” or words to that effect,
with true comic-opera enthusiasm.
Dear Bulletin, —Before the amalgamation
of the legal professions in Victoria, a barrister
was—for what reason nobody knows—supposed to
be the only person competent to draw pleadings
in an action. For instance, in an action for
simple recovery of money for goods sold, after writ
has been issued, appearance entered, and state-
ment, setting forth fully the grounds of claim, has
been filed by the plaintiff, the defendant has to
reply. This reply is almost invariably as follows :
“ The defendant denies that lie is indebted as
alleged.” These nine words cause the following
items to appear in the defendant’s solicitor’s bill
of costs :
£ s. cl.
Instructions for defence to statement of claim .. 0 S
Drawing aqd engrossing instructions to counsel
to draw same C fos. .. .. .. 12 0
Attending counsel therewith .. .. .. OS
Paid his fee and clerk .. .. ~240
Attending him receiving defence .. .. 0 8
Engrossing same .. .. .. .. 2 0
Attending tiling same .. .. .. OS
Paid .. 10
Attending plaintiff’s solicitor with copy .. 0 8
This item alone costing £4 12 10
Now, why on earth could not the solicitor run off
this little formula himself and charge his client,
say, half-a-sovereign, which would bo adequate
remuneration? Also, is the same state of things
to continue under amalgamation, and is one
amalgam to employ another at his client’s expense
tod) the work which lie is employed to do him-
self ? In the event of a person employing a
solicitor, should not that solicitor do all the neces-
sary work fur his client, and if he himself is nob
competent-as he should be--to write the afore-
said nine words at over a shilling apiece and sends
them on to someone else to write—should- not the
solicitor himself be forced by rules of court to pay
for the wot k ? Bmilkil

81C Macquavie-street North, Sydney, July 6,1592.
My Dkar Moorabinda, —
The annual ball in aid of the well-managed School of
Industry was a greater success this year than last, when—
as every butterfly remembers—only a couple of hundred
people turned up. At the plain and fancy dress ball in
•Centennial Hall on 29th ult., there must have been thou-
sands present—but I am not skilful at counting crowds,
especially when some of their ingredients come out in two
half-suits of clothes, and, consequently, look like two
people instead of one. Which greatly adds to the nume-
j ical difficulty of the case - especially after supper.
Wigs, paint (plenty of it, daubed on openly, as is per-
missible at costume balls, and don’t the women make the
most of it !) and powder, transformed folks, so that “ Is it
Mrs.’Arris?” was heard on every side, and the diffident
people in or’nery dress were quite a rest after one had
been “guessing again” in front of masked individuals,
with scaly paws and tails, and all sorts of horrors. But
the fancy dresses were the most interesting—especially
w hen the wearer wisely refrained from revealing too much
«'f her scaffolding, so to speak. Only an actress can wear
u knee-cap skirtette and look pleased and happy at the
same time. When a private person, in a burst of Dutch
courage at her dressmaker’s, orders too many tucks in her
hilt, she becomes a prey to burning embarrassment from
the moment she steps on to the floor. And bending
forward—the usual trick of the woman who is conscious
of too much ankle—isn’t the slightest use, for what you
gain in length in front, you lose very pointedly in the
roar.
I didn’t see any prettiest costumes, because they were
mostly very well done, except that some of the wigs sat
gingerly on misfit crania, and appeared as if combed out
with a rake. Miss Simpson was very thoroughly got up
i i “ stars and stripes”; Miss Isabel Osborne’s gipsy dress
was carried out to walnut-juice lengths ;Miss L. Osborne’s
“Haymaker” dress became her to the last tooth in her
rake; Miss Marcia Cox was well disguised as “Pink
Domino Dr. Arthur Cox, “ Black Domino Mr. A. W_
Nathan wore diamond buckles on his “David Garrick”
fchoes. There was a Devil hailing from Gunnedah, and
somebody (a S3*dney surveyor, who, by some means, was
credited with being identical with the brother of a well-
J.nown athletic solicitor) was really funny as an old Roman
coryphee—scarlet tights and yellow gauze skirts, but he
wouldn’t take off his top-wrap, and there isn’t much fun
about the outer aspect of an overcoat that I can see. Sir
John Falstaff spoilt a correct get-up by omitting to pad
his paunch ; and Mons. Marius and Otho Stuart turned up
in evening dress. They’re so tired of the “ other thing ”
1 hat no doubt a plain boiled shirt and swallow-tail are a
perfect joy to them.
Powdered hair was very general among the ladies who
weren’t in costume. Mrs. Burdskin, in black merv. with
lilac bodice and diamond necklet, wore her hair powdered;
so did Miss Burdekin, in black lace with trimming round
the corsage of pink unmounted roses. Miss Amy Roberts
looked well in cream brocade and lace; Mrs. Bloomer
wore a dainty confection of sea-blue armure royale and
chiffon, diamond ornaments ; Mrs. J. R. Fairfax combined
dark-grey and slate-grey brocade. And soon. Floor and
music were lively, but the “costume sets” were spoilt
by the whole ball joining in. The Shakespearean set,
however, kept sui generis —and let me add in conclusion
that the eleven o’clock supper was a worthy effort
decorated with jonquils.
Listen for a moment. At a fancy-dress ball, do you
think it politic for the painfully “uninteresting’’ young
man with scarlet hair and yellow teeth to go in ordinary
evening dress, and tell enquiring friends that he’s just )
come as a “plain gentleman?” I don’t think so.
What you might call Miss Bessie Doyle’s private view s
came off at Paling’s rooms on last Saturday afternoon in ■'
the presence of a select mob of top-sparklers, including
Madame Kellermann, Poussard, Hazon—but you know
the list. The “view’’was a complete success from tile-
opening concerto to the Barcarole of Hans Stilt, which
was ’thusiastically encored in a manner that predicted
S.R.O. houses for the whole of the Doyle season.
Even if Miss Bessie could not fiddle like fun, you could
not want your money back when she once made her
curtsey. There is more than a good stage presence and a
pretty face—there is a suggestion of dreamy soulfulness
about her, and in looking upon Our Own fiddleress one
feels a comforting conviction that she couldn’t, under
any circumstances, have a weakness for corned beef and
cabbage. On Saturday the picture she and her fiddle
made, quite transfixed me. The night was poetically
gloomy, and the only light was over her head. She is o ’
medium height and slender, with dark hair and ’witching-
eyes, and her dress was very plainly made—of black silky
stuff cut to a train with an odd sort of jewel-clasp, and a
happy-go-lucky bunch of white Marguerites for orna-
ments. As she stood, obviously very nervous, gracefully
preparing to astonish us, she reminded me of some
artistic pictures one happens on in the magazines. To
descend to plain figures, Miss I).’s playing is a satisfying
thing, and she puts enough real soul into it to keep every
true lover of burlesque and the ballet miles away from her
show. I shall be interested to see how many of the other
sort there arc in Sydney.
Apropos music, Miss Julio Hack’s concert, the booking
for which was very heavy in the Potts Point direction,
occurred too late for notice this week. Next week my
g rose-quill goes into the honey on the subject.
1 That rather lovely breach-of-promise case recently
before a Southern law-court had one or two new features.
J-'or one, it was rather unusual to find that it was a man
who enlightened the defendant on the naughtiness of the
plaintiff. Generally it’s a veiled and mysterious female
with a sinister expression to her very hair-pins, who get»
the trusting lover by the ear and turns the other lady’s
dream of joy into a horrible nightmare ; but for a man lo
drop darkling hints as to the moral rectitude of another
man’s fiancee is quite a novelty. As a rule, the short-
haired half of humanity don’t exactly telephone for
Nemesis to drop upon a woman, for some reason or other
—conscience, perhaps ; but still they don’t.
Another phase of the 8.P.-suit might be whistled to
“Tommy make room for your uncle,” and shows how
difficult it is to know whether the charming girl who goes
about with her “little niece” is really that engaging
infant’s aunt or whether she is a total stranger brandish-
ing a mock relation in the eye of society ; or, deeper still’
whether she is modestly disclaiming her own handiwork
for various reasons ?
Talking about B.P. suits—there were several glimpses
of woman-nature in the suit, recently before a Sydney
court, brought by an East Indian against his recalcitrant
charmer. The gentleman, it appeared, had paid her passage
to Australia, and given her many presents before she left.
When he arrived in Australia he had some difficulty to
find the defendant. When he did find her she was quite a
different woman. “ Before she always run and kissee me.
This time she ask me sit down, my lordship.” She kissed
him, you see, before her passage was paid, and she offered
him a chair afterwards! Such is life—and love ! She
lost the case and has to pay the costs—and go to gaol
unless the costs be paid! But he says, poor, infatuated
wretch, that he is still willing to marry her. If she
marries him, leaves him at the church-door and stalks off
under the protection of an amateur pugilist, her husband
can’t put her in gaol. Happy thought!
By the way, do women value men at a higher figure
than that at which men assess women ? It would be a
t milling subject for a debating society with not too many
young orators. Meanwhile, in the absence of discussion,
the humiliating idea that the case stands as put is forced
upon me by the fact that in the breaches-of-promise
which have amused us recently the blighted lady made her
faithless lover’s valuation tot up three thousand pounds !
In the other case the male plaintiff unflatteringly con-
sidered £2OO was compensation for what he had lost !
Women at large will probably never learn sense, and
while there are petticoated female idiots who sneak out to
attend a rendezvous arranged in a newspaper with a man
of whom they know absolutely nothing we shall have
duplicate Kate Rounsevelles with all the risks that
courageous lady ran. Here are two promising advertise-
ments coming one on top of the other in the good Sydney
Telegraph, which treated us all to such a chaste sermon
on newspaper ethics, the other day ;

The late Mister Deeming sometimes wore a white vest
and probably looked just as harmless as “gent in straw
hat ” until he got before a jury. And Deeming’s smile
was considered one of that hero’s chiefest attractions, I
believe, and his most potent weapon of conquest. Can
my dear sisters have forgotten the Awful Warning (in big
capitals) against dalliance with unknown admirers which
that gentleman’s adventures furnished such a little while
ago?
Lord and Lady Jersey presided at a recent nice ing of
Sydney City Mission at Waterloo, and, of course, both the
“crowned heads” had a little say. Lady J. (in semi-
mourning) made a trite speechlet which, viewed strictly
from a Waterloo standpoint, was fitfully amusing. The
titled speaker advised the mothers present to teach their
children obedience and helpfulness—“to be polite; to
re juest, not to order,” and so on—lovely copy-book conn
sellings of the highest grade, of course ; but, it’s ail very
well to talk—just think of the life a “polite gutter-
child ” would lead in Waterloo ! And it’s a question, too,
whether the local mamma knows exactly what childish
politeness consists of herself. She was probably brought
up by the hair and she drags her family up according to
hereditary instinct. When you tell her to teach Temmy
to be “ polite ” the only result is that she takes two twists
instead of one in his startled red hair and bangs his skull
twice as hard against the chimbley. Lady Jersey’s refined
counsels, intrinsically commendable as they were, struck
me at the time as being on a par with the thoughtfulness
of the Christian society girl who insists on teaching the
labouring man’s wife how to make a Russian salad.
Lord Jersey’s speech on this occasion was decidedly re-
assuring and settled a question that has long been
agitating society at large. In concluding, the good-
natured Gov. assured the audience that, though sometimes
forced to put on splendid clothes and wear an uncomfort-
able cocked hat, yet “he teas only human like them-
selves.” This is the sort of information that never comes
too late.
Lord Jersey had rather an interesting experience at
Wentworth when he was dodging round that settlement
on his last back-blocks picnic. When he arose to depart
he went genially round the town to bid good-bye to some,
,if not all, of his hospitable entertainers. But lo ! every
one of them was too inarticulate in the feet to be able to
stand up without the help of a steam crane, so he aban-
doned the farewell idea and got away unsquoze. The only
sober man he found in the town was a Quaker!
I regret extremely that last week I solidified into a
paragraph a floating rumour that a Sydney medical man’s
two children had hurt themselves “playing at Deeming.”
As a matter of fact (which is always what I aim at) the
story is only a baseless and probably a malicious rumour,
and the youngsters are really as right as ninepence.
Relationships— pur et simple—are always puzzling to an
outsider, but when you come to think how often the
unctuous old gentleman with the paternal manner isn’t
the “ uncle ”of his pink-cheeked small-waisted “ niece ’
and with what monotonous frequency it turns out that
nobody died and left the charming golden-haired girl as a
solemn trust to a “guardian ” about five years her junior
—when one revolves the frequent perversions of lawful
connections one secs, to believe anybody even on the
identity of his grandmother (if she happens to have one
tooth in more than is compatible with the circumstances
of the case) is to do a silly thing.
Mr. Alfred Nathan (of Macnamara & Nathan, solicitors),
leaves Sydney soon on a trip to Europe, where some of
the time will go in putting a lasting shine on his vocal
register. Going to study music on the Continong, in
short.
By the way, people are asking what it means when a
charming and well-pursed young widow always trots
round the Block with a parson in tow? Does it mean that
the parson is the widow’s anointed as it were? or does
she only keep him handy in case she makes up her mind
in a hurry ?
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

Alexandra Dramatic Coy.
After a week of misfortune, “ The Whirli-
gig” has retired from Sydney Opera House, and
given place to “A Pleasant Home,” a somewhat
less distressful comedy in four acts. The motive
of this new piece is obscure and foggy, and the
plot is as disjointed as a torn lobster, but the first
duty of a comedy is to be amusing, and the
“ Pleasant Home ” is amusing a great part of the
time. It is based upon an uncle who goes away
from home for reasons that are not very well
defined—because the uncle is in full shriek the
instant the curtain rises and hasn’t a chance to
explain—and leaves a racketty nephew in charge
of the house, and the nephew straightway calls in
a bad character with a red head, and they turn
the mansion into a boarding-house. The boarding-
house isn’t a success, and presently the premises
seem to be given over to hogs, dogs, and devils
and the great What-is-it. There are always cats
going mad on the stairs, and the galvanic
boarder having fits and porous plasters in
the back parlour, and the invisible boarder
howling wretchedly on the second floor, and the
leg-show boarder bounding out of her room in
tights, and crashing up against the old, pious
boarder, and the bailiffs are after the boarder
with the eye-glass, and the insane boarder swallows
an car-trumpet or something, and has paroxysms
among the furniture to the horror of the peaceful
fat boarder. There are 700 doors in the piece—
more or less—and a boarder is concealed behind
each of them, and if he is seen there he will be
lost, and another bot tler is trying to burst in, and
if he can’t get in be will be found, and if he is
f mud lie will be ruined. No one knows why all
CHARLES BRADLEY.
these tilings are except possibly the demented
boarder, who has the wild, haggard look of a man
that is about to start a newspaper in the
bush, and the iron-jawed wife of the fat
boarder who thinks her husband is in love with
the boarder in tights. There are cigar-ashes
and torn letters all over the place, and somebody
is always arriving in a breathless state at the
front door, and, finally, the uncle comes back,
apparently off his head, and meets all these
boarders, and is considerably astonished. Then
the uncle lives happily ever afterwards, so far as
we are aware, and the insane boarder comes out
and has a last fit on the sofa, and the fat boarder
is forgiven, and the misunderstanding with the
boarder in tights is cleared up in time to prevent
the invisible boarder upstairs wrecking the whole
establishment, and the other characters either
break their necks on the stair, or make a fortune
in silver scrip, or something.
# * *
There is no well-defined heroine in this
amazing show. Miss Bella Bashall as the boarder
in tights takes deservedly a large share of the
applause, and a very little is left over for Mrs.
Bennett. But what ought to be the centre of the
piece is the insane boarder as played by Mr.
Poulton—-a character that is one-half “ the Private
Secretary ” and one-half galvanic monkey prancing
up a yellow stick, and both halves supremely
ludicrous. Instead of this, however, the author's
great effort is to make the play revolve round Mr.
J. R. Greville, the fat boarder, and this puts it
somewhat out of joint. Messrs. F. J. Currier,
Shine, and Lash Gordon are the other prominent
features, but inasmuch as they are compelled, by
the exigencies of the comedy, to tear across the
stage at full gallop most of the time they don't
display any features of particular interest. “A
Pleasant Home ” isn't a restful production. With
the exception of the fat boarder, hardly anybody
has a moment's leisure, and when the leading
characters find a sofa or a mahogany table in the
way they generally rush through it for want of
time to go round. This undue haste makes the
boarders look unnatural at times, but otherwise it
does no particular harm. Next Saturday, Miss
Jennie Lee starts for a season of one week in the
ever-welcome character of “Jo,” and her well-
worn broom which has swept so many crossings
will be again in evidence.

“ The Merchant of Venice ” boomed at
Sydney Garrick on Saturday night amid all the
enthusiasm which the little theatre could hold.
As Shylock, the stately Hebrew who discounted
Antonio’s “ beel ” in Venice, Mr. Bentley was at
his best, and his Jew was a memorable success.
There was nothing small or grovelling about the
dramatic usurer, nothing even faintly suggestive
of the retail pawnbroking industry, or the bill-of-
sale over the furniture of two rooms and a
kitchen; he was a big;, masterful Jew from first
to last—the ideal Semite who is so often heard of
and so seldom met with in real life, built after
the probable fashion of the great original Israelite
—the hook-nosed spearman whose burnished beak
reflected the light of the setting sun as his tribe
looked down on doomed Palestine away back in the
year 2, or thereabouts. If that legendary plunderer
ever existed, Shylock is his double, and if he never
existed at all and Palestine was merely seized
under a mortgage then Shylock is intended to
represent him as he ought to have been but wasn’t.
Walter Bentley’s Hebrew was exceedingly real,
and when he slowly polished up his butcher’s
knife in the trial-scene and ground the edge on his
capacious shoe he was a cheerful reminiscence of
the Psalmist when he was out chopping up Philis-
tines Jin the name of the old war-god of Judah.
Shylock himself was the soul of the drama, and so
long as he was there the audience didn’t care
whether Antonio was cut up or not, or whether
Gratiano and the rest were massacred along with
him. Miss Hansen’s Portia was pretty and in-
telligent and elagently dressed, but there were no
very strong points about her; and Jessica, per-
petrated by Miss Grace Hamilton, was a
nice-looking Jewess whoso mission was obviously
fulfilled when she bolted with a Venetian dude.
The merchant —by our esteemed friend, Bill South
—seemed an honourable and upright man whose
only fault was that his voice came from theabysse s
of his boots ; and George
Coppin, junior, played
the part of the frowsy
Hebrew, Tubal, to con-
siderable ad vantage.
A Iso, Bassanio and
Gobbo, as delineated by
Messrs. Roberts and
Clitlierow, were suffici-
ently good. Mr. Bent-
ley benefits on Friday, in
“ Othello ” —for the first
and last time in Sydney
—and then lie proposes
to pack up his ruined
Hebrew and his other
properties, and leave for
brighter scenes.

The past week at
Sydney Criterion has
been one of shreds and
patches. “Adrienne
Lecouvreur” was suc-

“Masks and Faces,”
which ran with much

ance ” was put up on
Monday before a crowded
audience, and when it
was finished the ques-
tion whether it was
writte. by Boucicault or
the other fellow was left
as unsettled as before,
and the ghosts of the
two dead authors pro-
bably fought the matter
out in the deserted build-
ing when the audience
was gone, and the lights
were out, and the graves
gave up their dead.
“ Fedora’’was announced
for Tuesday ivght as the
last tragedy of Mrs.
Beere’s season, and her last opportunity of
demonstrating to the people of Sydney that
this world is but a fleeting show, and that
everything is out of joint, and it would
be better for mankind in general that they
had never been born. “The School for Scandal,”
for one night only, and “ Masks and Faces,” put
up on Thursday for Mrs. Beere’s benefit, close the
proceedings. Then the Sydney johnny will heave
his last ill-directed bouquet, and the great
tragedienne will depart, and unless the Bibbs
Government makes some considerable improve-
ments in the tariff next session, and closer super-
vision is exercised in the winding-up of Abigail’s
bank, and the financial institutions of N.S. Wales
are required to make a solid deposit with the
Government as security for their creditors, Heaven
only knows when that province can afford to
look upon her like again.
** * «
As for the other assorted shows of
Sydney, some of them have no flies upon
them, and some have. The Gettysburg Cycle-
rama is much as usual, and time writes no wrinkle
on the azure brow of Dan Tracey, who holds forth
at the School of Arts with bi.a customai y ability.
Miss Bessie Doyle, “our own violinist,” is
announced to give two concerts at the Centenary
Hall, under the patronage of the Bench and
the Ministry, the consuls, musical societies, and
“the Army,"whatever that may be — The Bulletin
didn’t really know there was one. Also, on July
14, Carl Hertz, the magician and illusionist, will
commence a two weeks’ season at the Garrick,
and will cause people and things to disappear
unaccountably. If it is also within the resources
of his art to bring back sundry people who have
already disappeared and are much wanted by the
depositors, he will leave a name behind him that
will not soon be forgotten.

The friends —and they are innumerable—
f Mr. G. L. Goodman, acting-manager of the
“busted Sydney Royal, have decided to present
with a testimonial. Mr. Goodman has been
connected with the Royal for 15 years, and all
that time he has been a man and a brother in the
highest sense of the term ; therefore this little,
benefit should be a great success.
The above is intended to explain, in a sympa-
thetic, Shakespearean way, that George Rignold
has brought his clever show to Melbourne at a
bare, bleak time, when hunger stalks in the
market-place and the imaginative faculty is trying
to devise a means of meeting bills which are full
of stern fact. George’s bill, being of the immortal
poetical order, attracted but a meagre attendance
to the Princess’. The rising generation of foot-
ballers, who had never seen “ A Midsummer
Night's Dream ” in the flesh, nor troubled
to wrestle with that beautiful play id the
spirit, showed no desire to make its acquaint-
ance on Saturday. The artistic loss will
be the public’s if they continue to stay
away, but the financial loss will fall upon the
management, and necessitate a speedy withdrawal
of the Bard’s poem in favour of a mundane drama
with a tank in it. This is a consummation
devoutly to be regretted. “A Midsummer
Night’s Dream,” as here presented on the stage, is
a charming show in some places and a sufficiently
funny comedy in others. There are moments
when it loses touch with earthly things and gently
lifts the dullest spectator into a world of phantasy;
as, for instance, when Titania woos the translated
weaver and falls a-slumbering with his donkey-
head against her cheek. A more convincing
realisation of the impossible is seldom attained
behind the footlights. Throughout this scene,
which is a triumph of stage painting and weird
effects, Miss Spong plays her part of the illusion
for all it is worth. She becomes a fairy down to
the feet, but more especially about the arms, and
her affection for the monster seems both warm
and spiritual at once.
The Shakespearean influence is asserting itself
considerably in this notice, but no matter. George
Rignold’s performance of Bottom, the weaver, has
spoken for itself many a time and oft, and needs
no more bush just now. Of the other comic
Athenians, good old Stirling Whyte seems more
sterling than ever, whilst among the mortal hong
long, Cosgrove is the least open to complaint.
Little Mabel Bussell is a pleasant surprise as
Puck, but several other performers are more sur-
prising than pleasant, and may bo the subject of
mild protest in our next. The ankles of the in-
cidental fairies present an earthly appearance,
and three sprites, who come and disappear in a
mystic manner, are otherwise ordinary atrocities,
and might be dispensed with.
Hi * * *
At Melbourne Royal there was a big
Saturday-house for “The Trumpet Call,” and a
disposition to take patches of that melancholy
waste of money in a serious spirit. Miss Edith
Blaude’s vigorous acting, in the dissolute char-
acter of the woman who always turns up when she
is thought to be dead, relieved the monotony of the
pageant. Also, Miss Kate Bishop put a quantity
of feeling into a lot of unnecessary distress, and
the Bland Holts rushed to the “comic relief,”
with untiring energy, and the rest of the company
tried hard to make folks believe that “The
Trumpet Call ” is a panorama with a purpose.
But it isn’t. It is merely a dull, expensive narra-
tive interspersed with soldiers, and the rising Aus-
tralian refuses to have his mind excited by roars and
rumours and wars upon the stage. The hero who
hurls himself into a campaign and crawls up the
cannon’s mouth in order to banish bitter memories
an exhausted volcano, and the widowed wife
who thinks her husband perished in the engage-
ment where he didn’t perish at all has lost much
of her original interest,

Matters elsewhere are flourishing and
otherwise. The Italians drew a crowd to the
Exhibition concert on Saturday last, and are
appearing again during the week. Coghill
Brothers make money at St. George’s Hall, but

Through the sweet Midsummer Night
One may dream of elf and sprite,
Mystic forms of fancy born.
Dancing in the pale moonlight—
Dream the Dream of Bard sublime
In the drowsy summer time,
When the cricket midst the corn
Seems to sing an impish rhyme.
But when night falls cold and chill
Over dale and over hill,
Fairy fictions withered die,
Winter frosts unkindly kill
Tender flowers of poetry.
Thus the burden of our song.
Though warbled vainly,
When the Fairy Hilda Spong
Strokes those ears so round and long,
And loves insanely
Beast ungainly—
“ Trip, trip, trip, upon the grass,
Would we were that other ass ! ”
At Poverty Point.
When Deeming’s Eainhill cottage was being re-erected
in the chamber of horrors at Tussaud’s Waxworks, the
workman employed on the job fell down dead among the
debris. Deeming evidently means to be fatal to every-
body who had anything to do with him. Even the man

The other night a certain Sydney theatre was much

dated with chairs placed in the passages. As the audi-

Priscilla Hugo obtained a license to quit her husband,
“ Charles” Hugo, at Melbourne Divorce Court, last week.
His Honor found Chawles guilty of habitual drunkenness
and cruelty, whilst casually remarkingthat his reputation
as an artist was not assailed. In fact, his Honor almost
suggested that a public entertainer is all the better for
“ going on the booze.”
The humane Leumane has come forward with a pro-
posal to arrange a series of concerts for the benefit of
Melbourne unemployed. The scheme suggested by this
benevolent tenor is sound and simple. If the various
suburban corporations will provide a free town-hall,
piano, printing and advertising (which wouldn’t need to
be extensive) a number of artists will give their services
towards a couple of entertainments per week, and a
representative of the Age can act as treasurer and pay in
the entire receipts to the office-fund.
Some hopeless crank writes thus of the fat, greasy,
commonplace Oscar Wilde, as a musician;—“This is
astral music ; and if he who wrote it has not lived afore-
time in Egypt and in Syria, in violet-crowned Athens and
in Dante’s own city of flowers, I have misunderstood the
music. I prefer to think that he has haunted some black
colossal temple of Isis, a pure priest in saffron robes, and
looked into the eyes of the sphinxes with eyes as long and
subtle as their own ; that he walked with the Saviour
among the lilies of the field in Palestine ; that he walked
with Sophocles in white Colonus, or listened to Agathon’s
rose-red praise of love and joy with Socrates at the sym-
posium that Plato tells of. He has beheld the Phoenix
rise from its ashes, and knows the meaning of it all; he
has worshipped the ibis and the serpent, and tenderly
buried the bones of dead kine in the isle of Prosopis ; he
has known the rites of Melita ; he has seated himself at a
table in Galilee and broken bread with the Lord ; he has
fed the sparrows of Aphrodite in the temple precinct of
her marble abode in Paphos.” Who can drivel like the
British critic ?
Our Mr. Cartwright, who is playing Cromwell in Sims
and Buchanan’s new historical drama of “The White
Rose,” at London Adelphi, is described as making the
Protector “a tender and pathetic old gentleman—so like
an old lady.”
Englishers have a noble opinion of Australia. In the
“ new ” English play, “The Fringe of Society ” (founded
on an old French one, Dumas’ “Demi-Monde”), the
heroine, Mrs. Eve-Alien, has been divorced from her first
husband, but does not choose to mention that fact when
she is seeking to entrap into marriage an Australian
millionaire, Arthur Cuthbert. Says a reviewer in a
London journal:—“Surely the fact that Mrs. Eve-Alien
is only ‘ on the fringe of society ’ would matter little in
the colonies to which her lover proposes to bear her off
directly after marriage.” "Anything’s good enough for
the kawlinies.”
Keiley, the Borsa in “ Rigoletto,” at Melb. Princess’, is
a musically worthy son of the late Henry Keiley, of Melb.
Argus.
Phil. Stuart’s newly-arrived tattooed man, “Captain
Fisher,” held an interesting Melbourne press-view leviie
the other day, and aired himself in a state of nature be-
tween two whisky-decanters and an asbestos stove. The
Captain told his romantic yarn in a plaintive tone of long-
suffering resignation, until the lady representative of a
Melbourne paper came prancing in “ to see your new
curiosity, Mr. Stuart.” When the tatui looked at her
with the air of a dashing militaire of the old school, and
she saw that he had no clothes on, she fled shrieking from
the ro'om, and the Tattooed Man laughed right down to his
nethermost vignette. “Lady reporters” ought to take
out a surgery degree before they shove in everywhere
after the men.
In course of an action against H. M. Stanley by
one Pewtress, a lecturers’ agent, who ran the brigand’s
English lecturing tour, Pewtress “would not say that
the tour was a imseiable frost, but it was a bad specula-
tion. He lost £7OO by the lectures.”
Mr. “Atkinson” (young Fittes, of Melbourne), “the
Australian Orpheus,” as he is called, has just entered into
a very good engagement to perform in Paris, Berlin,
Vienna, and other continental cities, at a big salary. The
engagement will extend over six months or more.
Janet Achurch, writes The Bulletin correspondent, was
badly advised in challenging comparison between herself
and Miss Ward in “Forget-Me-Not,” which was put up at
London Avenue vice “A Doll’s House,” which this time
quite failed. Miss A. did her very best, but she was not
equal to the part, her mannerisms of voice being pain-
fully evident. She had, however, some good moments,
and cannot be said to have failed altogether. As for poor,
ponderous Charrington, the less said the better. He has
rarely been seen to worse advantage (and that means a lot)
than in the character of Sir Horace Welby so identified
with the name of W. H. Vernon.
Mr. Pigott, the British Examiner of Plays, is no
Ibsenite. In his evidence before the Commons Committee
on Theatres he said that “he had studied Ibsen’s plays
carefully, and he thought all the characters were morally
deranged. All the heroines were either dissatisfied
spinsters who looked upon matrimony as a sort of
monopoly, or married women who were in a chronic state
of rebellion against all conditions that nature had im-
posed upon their sex, or against the social and moral
restraints applicable to wives and mothers. All the main
characters are either rascals or imbeciles.” Ibsen has
probably never been so concisely summed up before.
In Vienna recently a huge benefit performance was
given on behalf of the Actors’ Benevolent Fund. All the
characters were inverted, the tragedian doing low comedy,
and the tragedienne singing a comic song, while the funny
men all took the tragic parts, and so on. How would
such a show go in Australia, the performance consisting
of scraps from half a dozen plays, with J. C. Williamson
as Joseph Surface, George Rignold as the Widow O’Brien,
Robert Brough as Othello, Royce as Macbeth, and
Walter Bentley as Blueskin ? It should be worth seeing.
Looking at “ Joan of Arc,” here is an idea: the theatres
make up their pieces all of music-hall business, and then
complain if the music-halls pick out little bits of drama.
Charrington wouldn’t let Flemming act Sir Horace
Welby in London.
Pigott, the Play Licenser, says the theatres gradually
purify as you go from the London West End to the East
End.

A musical piece, called “Soylla,” failed in one of the
Paris theatres a few weeks ago, and the tenor, M. Gerald,

All the Melbourne papers, in describing the production
of Wycherly’s “ Country Girl ” at the Bijou, mentioned
that the original was, from a modern standpoint, disgust-
ing and indecent. Since then, all the booksellers in
Melbourne have had numerous enquiries for a not too
much expurgated copy of that author’s plays, and there
has been a perfect rush on the Public Library. Dirt
always sells well.
At Hobart the management of a skating-rink fishes for
the elite by advertising one night in the week as “ strictly
fashionable.” On one of these “ fashionable ” evenings
the manager was requested by a sassiety young man to
remove a “feller” who was not of the magic circle, dont-
cherknow. The manager protested that the “feller”
was personally known to him and he could vouch for his
respectability. But he was told that respectability didn’t
count anyhow—“ the ladies indignantly refuse to skate in
the same room with a man who serves in a shop !” The
young man had to go.
