The Wild-Cat Column.
The Broken Hill lock-out is a godsend to those
responsible for the woeful condition of the big mine,
which may fall in at any moment. A manager of big
reputation who inspected the Proprietary mine last year
for the first time, declared it to be one of the richest and
most extravagantly-worked mines in the world, and that
with all the extravagance in soft-wood timbering, &c., it
would probably collapse within a year. He saw an army
of men watching and repairing the square-sets, another
army was cutting them out, and above this yawning
cavern the whole of the machinery-thousands of tons of
it—rested on the hanging-wall side of the lode. The
mine, he said, might have been worked at half the outlay,
and had the ore taken out been put back as slag the
place would have been safe enough. To do this now is
quite another matter, but something of the sort will have
to be done or the mine abandoned.
* * •
Apart from the question whether it is better to
smelt locally or at Port Pirie, the price (£26,000) to be
paid by the Proprietary for the British smelters is a pro-
digious one, but then it has been the rule of the Proprie-
tary to give the very biggest money for everything. The
miners are now asked to pay the difference between
economy and extravagance.
* # *
The attempts made to reduce the earnings of the
Barrier and Tasmanian miners follow in the natural order
of things. On the Barrier it is the effect of extravagant
and incompetent management, of which, of course, the
miners are to be the victims. In Tasmania it is the effect
of a wild boom of valueless silver mines. Because tens
of thousands have been squandered on barren ground,
and hundreds of thousands lost in share gambling, the
miners are asked to pull the mines through by a lower
rate of wage.
m * *
A man who is probably the largest shareholder
in the biggest of mines, has openly accused certain of the
directors of working the mine to suit themselves, adding
that he held more shares than all the directors put
together, and that more than one of them had to borrow
shares to maintain his qualification.
* * *
With what wonderful unanimity publicity is
given to the “ serious state of things ” in the Proprietary
mine now that the stock has depreciated millions! Months
ago there appeared in this column a two-line paragraph
to the effect that the “creep” in the mine was nothing
compared to the creep out of it. Nor did we minimise
the seriousness of the creeps. From time to time there
was published a warning-note that the big mine could not
rest on timber, and unless filled in with its slag it would
one day fill itself in. This great danger was apparent to
every miner employed in it. It must have been known
by the management. It was periodically denied, and even
up to a fortnight ago the newspapers were full of assur-
ances of safety under such headings as a “Slight Co-
llapse,” &c. A main shaft since abandoned as absolutely
wrecked was described as “ having had its timbers slightly
disturbed.” The open-cut system was proclaimed as the
new economical method of working, and with such reitera-
tion that the poorpublic believed it. As a matter of fact,
it was only done to prevent the mine collapsing. Never-
theless the shares continued to fall under the “creep-
out,” and now that they are almost down to zero we have
at last the truth about the “huge fissure,” “ the deplor-
able state of the timbers,” “the giving way of the
shafts,” and the prediction of an early fall-in.
* * *
Baker’s Creek mine lias, since its start four and
a half years ago, raised 20,000 tons for 80,000 ozs. of gold.
Somewhat remarkable that the first gold bearing reef
found in that tremendous gorge where there are many
others, should bo better than all the others put together.
It is a magnificent shoot of gold contained in a reef which
does not average six inches.

* # ft
Hillgrove wants N.S.W. Government to send
parties out to prospect the surrounding ranges and
gorges. Tub Bulletin trusts the Government will
decline. No doubt there are “other ricli reefs in the
district,” hut if they cannot be found without State aid,
they had better be left for a future and more enterprising
generation. Jlining is low enough, Heaven knows, hut
we don’t want to pauperise it. To control a private pros-
pecting party is difficult enough ; to send out State
parties would mean all sorts of villanies.
* # ft
North Johnstons —that grand old mine from
which tens of thousands of ozs. of gold have been raised
during the last quarter of a century is reported to he
“again on gold.” It so, we know what that means —
another period of dividends. There is nothing patchy
about its reefs, and nothing sensationally rich, hut a big
body of one-oz. rock.

been sold to an English syndicate, to he floated into a
£lOO,OOO company. We sincerely sympathise with the
syndicate, and hope the mine alone will he sold.

The Zeehan and Dundas smelters have been
running about a month, and have about the same time to
run, and then the ore supplies are down for a time (a
long time, we’re afraid). What the Dundas railway will
have to do then puzzles a good many. The w hole field
threatens to be one of the biggest of mining failures, and
Hobart, people are beginning to realise this.
ft ft ft
All the Pambula batteries are working for the
first time since their erection, and as several are running
on accumulated heaps, it will not he long before half of
them are again idle. After waiting many months for
some wonderfully cheap process of saving infinitesimally
fine gold, they have fallen back on ordinary battery-
treatment.

At last week’s meeting of the Mercantile
Finance Co. (Jlelb.) an attempt was made to discover
something about “Johanne Neilson,” figuring as the
holder of many thousand shares. The chairman admitted
that neither Neilson nor the original holder (for whom the
mysterious N. was a dummy seemingly) had paid the
recent call on all these shares, hut when asked to give
the name of the original holder the chairman said that
“ no good purpose would he served by disclosing it.” The
great financial policy of Melbourne directors is to be
solemnly mute about jobbery for which outside share-
holders have to suffer.
« ft ft
Attorney-General Barton has promised to amend
N.S.W. Bankruptcy Act so as to provide for the legalisa-
tion of private assignments representing four-fifths in
number and value of the creditors, as in the other Aus-
tralian States. Under the present N.S.W. law a can-
tankerous outsider can render the life of a debtor one of
perpetual misery, notwithstanding that the bulk of his
creditors have granted a full discharge.

or so, secured only on the hank’s own w orthless shares.
Agent-General Jlunro will likely be recalled to assist in
the investigation of the bank’s affairs.

Personal Items.
John Davies, C.M.G., M.L.C., is getting
bald.
Sir James Cockle, formerly Chief Justice,
draws an annual pension of <£1250.
An Adelaide medico owns a Sbradivarius
valued at <£Boo.
For the last eleven years there has only
been one S.A. Cabinet without a Wesleyan in it.
Speaker Coles, ex-Lands Minister Howe,
and Hopkins, of the S.A. Assembly, have all been
police-troopers.
Sir Patrick Buckley, the now M.L. knight,
was once a law-partner of Sir Charles (now Chief
Justice) Lilley at Brisbane.
The S.M. Hekald becomes absolutely
heretical. In its “Literary Gossip” it actually
criticises Queen Victoria’s grammar.
Apropos that absurd ceremony at Gordon’s
grave. You should just hear Mrs. Peter Low
(Gordon’s relict) on J. Hewlett Ross !
The story now goes that Speight has not
done well in silver. He made a big “rise” but
held on against the advice of his broker.
South Australia’s senior civil-servant is
Surveyor-General Goyder, who gets ,£1454 yearly
and lias held his present office for 31 years.
There is a doctor at Wellington (M.L.)
named Fell. Dr. Fell’s pa must have smiled to
himself as he apprenticed his boy to the doctoring.
P.M.G. Unmack is one of the ministers
of the notorious Queensland Cabinet whom the
Labour-party are “ plotting to murder.” Toper is
the other.
Adelaide has had two Anglican bishops in
50 years—Short and Ivennion. Melbourne has
had three, of whom two (Moorhouse and Goe) are
still living.
Dr. Cockburn, the S.A. Chief Secretary?
for years would not have a Bible in his house-
Now he looks upon it as a text-book and bulwark
of Socialism.
One of the standing nuisances of Sydney
is a man who asserts he is the son of perhaps the
most honourable man who ever sat iu the legis-
lature of Victoria.
Poor old ‘‘Augur” was a capable journalist,
so there is a grain of comfort in the thought that
he escaped the agony of reading his obituary
notice in Melbourne Sportsman.

Railway Commissioner Joe Smith (who has
wrecked two Ministries in succession), <£.500 a
year ; and Chief Justice Way, <£2ooo and fees.

as pioneer of the New Australian Socialist move-
ment, is “comping” on an English paper in
Buenos Ayres. The scheme is slowly maturing.

missioner of Works, is known as “Prince Bis-
marck” in Adelaide Assembly. There is supposed
to be a remarkable resemblance between the two.
Sala has already obtained for his little
penny weekly a circulation of 100,000. _ As it is an
inexpensive publication (Sala and his wife do
nearly all the work themselves) there is money iu
it.
An Australian journalist is preparing a
history of the Labour movement in Australia, to
the publisher’s orders, for inclusion in Swan
Sonnenschein’s well-known “Social Science”
series.
Jawbone Neild has been lecturing on his
Maoriland experiences, dating back to 1853. This
will help to dissipate the idea prevalent among
Sydney womankind that the man with the
moustache is only 34 years old.
An Australian ex-Cabinet Minister, who
has sadly fallen away, appeared, rolling drunk, the
other night, as a member of the Sydney unem-
ployed. He has long fed on husks, and been glad
to accept a bed at “ the Model.”

A. L. Gordon, was at the grave ceremonial last
Saturday. Gordon used to visit Whiteman's
forge, Maekillop-street, Melb., and recite his own
poem’s in a pleasing sing-song way.

The cost of Cardinal Manning’s funeral
had to be met by a subscription amongst bis
relatives and friends. Apart from the value of his
books, the good old man’s earthly possessions
didn’t amount to fourteen per cent, of his burial
expenses.

N.S. Wales.

Old Dean Macartney (C. of E.), Me -
bourne, who was born before this century was in-
augurated, is, as we write,-reported dying. He
saw the death of the Irish Parliament, of which
historic Assembly his father was a member, and
had hoped to live to see it re-established.
Phil. Nolan, sub-editor of the Buenos
Ayres Standard, is dead. He left Melbourne
for Argentina some fourteen years ago, firmly
convinced that no better opening for the exercise
of democratic principles than the corrupt and
priest-ridden Republic could be found elsewhere.

Chief Justice Way’s father was aPrimitive
Methodist parson, who, in the early days of South
Australia, peddled boots to eke out his ministerial
stipend. The Judge was a very dutiful son, and
prosperity did not spoil him in that respect, while
he still gives largely to the denominational funds.
He is a bachelor, but far outshines vice-regality in
hospitality.

“ He was an unostentatious and exemplary
Christian, and died in perfect peace and the faith of
the Gospel.” So runs the epitaph on John Probert,
of Auckland (M.L.), who left .£lOO to his dead-
broke brother, aged 72. and bequeathed <£26,000
to the Wesleyan Church as a big advertisement
for his corpse. “He was an unostentatious and
exemplary Christian.”
Wilkins— Mayor of Collingwood (Vic.)—
distributed gratis a quantity of superfluous meat
from his shop among the local unemployed before
the recent general elections. Wilkins —M.L. A.—
after the general elections, joined the butchering
ring which raised the price of meat to the public
before the Stock Act had come into operation.
Thus doth the wily capitalist laugh his enemies
into unspeakable scorn.
At the time of the sitting of the Federal
Convention in 1891, a prominent Australian legis-
lator then visiting London was introduced to Sir
R. G. W. Herbert, then, and until lately, Per-
manent Under-Secretary for the Colonies. The
Australian was interrogated as to Parkes’ real
motive for rushing Federation to the front at that
time, and said, bluntly, “ Another title!” “D—n
him !” said Herbert, “we've given him too much
already !”
That love-of-long-ago garland of English
wild-flowers was not allowed to remain long on
Gordon’s grave in Brighton cemetery. Two days
after the mysterious Air. Ross, who wears long
hair and a slouch hat (he’s also credited with a
blue plush mantle and dark lantern, but no
matter), had placed the wreath on the tomb, and
recited a jerky little tiling of his own, consisting,
for the most part, of “ O, poet, dead !” to a small
congregation which “knew Gordon, poor fellow,
well” the offering appeared in a Collins-street
bookseller’s window, backed up with portraits of
Mr. and Mrs. Gordon and an open book of the
poems. Romance!
J. K. MTntosh, the native-born Bathurst
solicitor who died last week, was (with the excep-
tion of B. G. Rodd, admitted in 1833, and still
alive) the oldest in the profession in N. S. Wales,
having been admitted, according to the Law list,
in 1840. He resided in Bathurst for nearly half a
century, served his articles with “Old” James
Norton, and in 1853 married the daughter of Mrs.
Black, who, atone time, kept the Royal Hotel,
Bathurst. It was at the corner of “Mother
Black’s ” lane that poor Deniehy was first picked
up, dying on his way to the hospital. MTntosh
prevented a pauper-funeral by paying all the
expense attending Deniehy’s interment.
Ex-Premier Atkinson, of Maoriland, fell
down dead the other day in Parliament House,
Wellington. The Bulletin’s last paragraph
about him was prophetic; his policy, while in
office in the days of the Continuous Ministry, Was
to break down the other members by sitting night
and day from the beginning of the session to the
end, and then run his measures through a House
that was too dead-tired to offer any opposition,
and he smashed himself up in the effort. His end
was ascribed to heart-disease, but he really died
of utter, unspeakable weariness. He was not a
great success as a statesman ; he worked in a
groove, and was a good, conscientious mediocrity
of the old type. But he was more honest than
the average of politicians, and never ran any
mysterious banks or got an overdraft in a way
that was calculated to arouse suspicion, and he
died poor.
Christie Murray, writes our London corres-
pondent, is at loggerheads with the Committee of
t’ne Savage Club. An attempt is being made to
raffle the celebrated portrait of E. J. Odell, by
Herkomer, for the benefit of the “Prince of
Bohemians,” who having suffered from some affec-
tion of the eyes for the past two years, is in a bad
way financially. Christie took the matter up and
addressed a circular to the members of the Savage
asking them to take tickets in the raffle. The
Committee think he was wrong in doing so, and
have passed a censure on him to which he will not
submit, and bo has demanded a special meeting
to discuss the matter. Whether he be right or
wrong there can be no two opinions as to the
action of the committee in taking the matter up
in such a spirit, for no one can doubt the good
motives which prompted the circular.

The Victorian butter bonus is lowered
from 3d. per lb. to l|d., possibly since the Govern-
ment has realised the fact that lately Maoriland
butter has been retailed in Melbourne at 2s. per lb.,
while best exported Victorian factory-made never
fetched more than 120 s, per cwt. There has been
more butter imported into the province than was
shipped from it, and the local consumer sadly
appreciates his idiocy in paying 3d. per lb. to see
bis best article forwarded to England while on his
own festive board rests a slab of mullock charitably
alluded to as “butterine !”

Among cur cue patent medicines vended in
Australia, there are few so largely in demand as
Clements Tonic. Despite the comparatively short
period that has elapsed since it first came before
the public, it has already practically supplanted
many older and apparently well-established reme-
dies, and judging by the vast number of testi-
monials published by the proprietor—testimonials
that are signed by persons within easy reach, and
whose statements can be verified if wanted—its
popularity would seem to be thoroughly well-
founded. Hitherto, Australians have been too
apt to trust to any medicine which came from the
farthest ends of the earth, and to despise every-
thing of local origin, but it is gratifying to note
that this tendency is passing away, and that a
remedy of local manufacture can stand before the
public on its own merits.
The Goode will case, which broke down
badly in N.S.W. Probate Court a few days ago, is
a romance after the manner of the three-volume
Yellowback, with a picture of the swarthy villain
and the shrieking heroine with her hair down
upon the front cover. Old Josiah (Joode, the
heavy father of the story, may be briefly described
as a rather dirty and drink-smelling old man with
property. He left his wife, or else she left him,
or else he threw her out, and after a time he
became the friend and protector of a certain un-
specified lady named Roland. How they became
acquainted isn’t stated-possibly Mrs. Roland
advertised in the News for a nice old gentleman
to lend her .£5. Then old Goode drank himself
off this earthly scene, leaving to his wife and
Madame Roland a life interest in his property—
the Dnbbo Hospital to annex the pile at their
death. Then ten years “rolled on”; Goode’s
widow married again, and her husband tried to
upset the will and failed. Then more years rolled
on, and one Maurice Barnett put an advertisement
in the paper to the effect that he had found
another will by the alcoholised Goode, leaving all
his property to his wife. More law ensued, Bar-
nett swore that he had bought a greasy, battered,
moth-eaten pile of Young Ladies’ Journals in
Paddy’s Market, Melbourne, and found Goode’s
will folded inside one leaf. He tried to fold the
leaf so as to show how a heavy legal document
might remain inside it unnoticed for 12 years and
not fall out, but he failed badly. There was no
evidence to show that Goode was addicted to
Young Ladies’ Journals, and the court apparent-
ly found it hard to imagine the bleary old
paralytic sitting over the fire and reading with
feverish interest about the Gwendolen who lived in
Ghost Grange and loved a curate. The will pur-
ported to have been signed and witnessed in a
pub. at Richmond ; two men who went in casually
for drinks one cold night found an old person
mumbling and chattering in an arm-chair, and he
asked them to sign a document, which they did
without asking any questions. Then Goode’s
doctor arose and swore that the venerable patient
was at home in bed that night, and couldn’t have
reached Richmond alive except in an ambulance.
Some one else arose and proved that Maurice
Barnett was an expert forger who had done various
terms in gaol. After that the case collapsed amid
very strong remarks from Judge Manning. The
weak point in the scheme stands out very con-
spicuously. Obviously the will shouldn’t have
been found by Barnett, whose character was not
good enough for him to discover such documents
successfully; it should have been planted in an
old book and the book left among the second-
hand stock in Cole’s Arcade, and then someone
of repute might have seen it and sworn to it as a
bona-fide discovery. If it had been put in a
volume of “ Lives of the Saints,” for instance, tile
chances are that a bishop Would have dropped on
it in the end, or a respectable priest whose
character could bear inspection, or it might have
been found by Father M'Kernan or tne Escaped
Nun. Having documents of that kind discovered
by a man who is only lately out of gaol is suicidal.

Since the M 4 Kinloy tariff became law the
exports of tho United States have increased by 85
millions of dollars. Under present
reciprocity arrangements, Great Britain is slowly
being squeezed out of the foreign markets in which
Uncle Sam is competing. The various European
Powers naturally prefer the secured market of the
States to the uncertain facilities supplied by the
groat three-ball shop.

testants of Ulster, and how they will perish in the

Orangemen are a minority even in Ulster, and all
they want, in a humble and godly way, is to rule,
not only the majority in their own province, but
all the rest of Ireland as well.

where the lost politicians go.

Many people seem to imagine, concerning
the “National Ass.,” which name is now the
universal designation for the bogus 4 National
Association of N.S. Wales, that the Ass. is a mere
abbreviation. This is wrong. * Ass isn t short tor
Association ; it is a separate word and is derived
from the hairy mule, on which Benjamin 1' ranklm
once rode to the poll to record his property-vote
in order to show the people that it was the quad-
ruped, and not ho, that really had the suffrage, ihe
mule, in fact, was hts qualification, and he brought
it along to prevent misconception.
According to local reports there are some-
thing like 14,000 Chinamen plodding over the
wastes of the Northern Territory in the hope or
sneaking into Queensland without paying the pod-
tax ; according to Griffith there are about 00 or
iOO. The truth lies somewhere between the two.
The country has dried up behind these miserable
Asiatics on their pilgrimage, and either Queens-
land must let them in or else they must go back
and leave their bones in the desert. There is rum
and leprosy in the one alternative and national
obloquy in the other. The cheap-labour curse is
coming home to roost.

ill per week with board and lodging on the sugar
plantations, and refused the terms, and, on tho
strength of this, the Tory press howls more loudly
than over about the horrors of the inflated and

205.; but the Queensland papers haven’t any mor-

The sweater-capitalist loveth the present
Government Labour Bureau, because their cardinal
article of faith is officially expressed in the “N.B.’
to their advertising placards ; “No delay for em-
ployers ; a large field open of all trades and callings
to select from.” Delay for employes doesn’t
matter. And the whole arrangement means that
the sweater-employer can “sack” squad after
squad of men at short or no notice until he gets
the cheapest in tho starving mob. And the one
grand maxim of a democratic community is loftily
declaimed to be that the people’s Government is
NOT bound to provide work for its unemployed.
The bogus- 44 Patriotic ” League and the
Labour-party in Queensland are silently mar-
shalling their forces for a tremendous struggle at
next general elections. Votes outnumber men
already in all the likely labour electorates. The
property, or what Benjamin Franklin called
the ass vote, is the reason. The Burke,
Bundamba, Charters Towers, Dalby, Drayton,
Toowoomba, Fortitude Valley, Gympie, Wool-
loongabba, and Nundah are all instances. On the
other hand, in the squatter electorates, there are
often three and four times as many men as votes.
The disfranchisement of shearers, etc., helps to
account for this. Balonne, Bullo, Carpentaria,
Clermont, Warrego, Flinders, and the Barcoo
are all glaring cases.
Patterson, the big, overbearing member
for Castlemaine (Vie.), said in the Assembly
recently that it was to be hoped the Government
would not find work for the unemployed, as such
a course would tend to pauperise the people. And
The Bulletin, which can be as brutal as Patter-
son when it feels inclined, devoutly hopes that if
that gentleman is ever run over by a tram-car, or
choked by a garrotter, the Government - as repre-
sented by the adjacent policeman—won't come to
his assistance lost that outdoor aid should pauperise
Patterson, don’t you know. Patterson was pauper-
ised once by the Government finding him a job in
the Ministry, and the effect was so bad that the
experiment should not be repeated.
When the Trades’ Disputes Conciliation
and Arbitration Act was passing through N.S.W.
Assembly it was argued by certain Labour mem-
bers that if the measure did not contain a compul-
sory clause it must prove abortive. The verity of
this was proved just as soon as the Act came into
use, when the Typographical Association offered
to submit a wage-lowering dispute to arbitration,
and tho daily newspaper proprietaries, wlo had
previously lauded the principle and the Bill to the
skies, promptly refused. The fact is that arbitra-
tion on the mutual-agreement basis was just as
possible before the Act was passed as now. The
employers of labour always were adverse to submit
disputes to arbitration, and are none the less
adverse now that a Bill has beem passed which
takes the matter in hand only after both parties to
the dispute have agreed to submit the matter to
the decision of a third party. The Act isn't worth
the paper it is printed on.
How shall be fought that other Waterloo?
There was he stayed, who would the world enchain,
Madly absorbed with selfish lust of gain,
Careless of misery. For he led the way
That others now adopt—a pirate crew
Who seize the needs of life, and with them make
A meaner warfare : And their fellows stake
As living counters in their wasteful game.
Where are the men, who dare their hearts obey—
Heeding not sullen hate, nor mocking leer—
And rid the world of this more monst rous fear
To give it so, a life of loftier aim?

How could I, irt a slavish land, accept my Six and-Eight?
“ Farewell! Farewell for ever 1 O Men of Brisbane Town,
Your Blackbird air contaminates my spotless legal gown ;
With South-Sea odours in the air, with prospects such as
these,
How could I scoop in costs at all—how could I take your
Fees ?
“ Farewell 1 My legal brethren. In legislative Halls
No more you’ll hear my silvern voice resound along the
walls—
So when you’re feeling * virtue’s gone,’ repress the rising
‘ Damn !’
Drop one sad tear into your beer, and say, ‘ Alas 1 for
Sam !’
“ Farewell 1 My bleeding country ! No nigger State for
me,
For Samuel’s boat is on the shore, his bark is on the sea ;
His wig and gown are packed away—his little hymn-book,
too—
Friends ! Do not weep for Samuel, but let him weep for
you.”
A tear rolled down his legal cheek and Wet his legal nose,
It trickled down his legal pants and wet his legal toes ;
It trickled down the pavement and the folk recoiled in
fear.
For who would brave the lion’s jaw or face the lawyer’s
tear?
Mount Morgan.

A yell-fed Victorian saint, whose fat salary
goes on all the same, land-boom or no land boom,
delivered himself the other day at a meeting of the
Y.M.C.A. as follows “ I began to fear that the
same awful lust for money that is crushing out tfija,
brightest and best of American life might obtain
here, but thank God for the depression ; thank God
for the sorrow. Out of it, I believe, will grow a
truer and nobler, life, and one full of blessing for
the young men around ua.” And then ho went
home to dinner and sat down to a feast of fat
things : of corn and honey and oil and wine—yea,
1 also beef and much gravy. ,
It may not be generally known that three Water-
loo veterans are ending their days in peace in Brisbane.
These gentlemen, whose united ages tiekrly total three
1 centuries (strictly speaking, 270 years), celebrated the
great victory by a little dinner on the 18th, the
anniversary of Waterloo day. The chair was occupied
by Mr. IF. Adams,, once a trumpeter in the King’s Own
Light Dragoons, now the Bth Hussars. Mr. Adams’
description of how the great battle was won lost none
of its fire, though the narrator was nearer 90 than 80.
The N.S. W. revenue returns for the past quarter show an increase over those
of the corresponding period of last year. The difference is £194,765. It is pretty
certain that advocates of trade restriction will regard this increase in the
revenue as an unmixed blessing. The introduction of a protective policy will
be credited with having placed the colony on the advantageous footing with
regard to public revenue which it enjoys as compared with most other coloniea
of the Australasian group. To this line of argument two very complete answers
may be given, etc., etc.— Sydney Free-Trade Daily.

Society
A certain Australian actor once quitted the
stage and took to the Law. He was a nice young
man with a cherubic expression and an eye-glass,
and he had played dude parts until a certain haw-
haw had become indelibly fixed in his constitution.
Therefore it came to pass, when he got his first
case, that he rose and delivered himself thus:
“Y’r Honor, I appoah faw the d’fendant in this
case ; its so awf’Jy jawlly, y’r Honor, doncher-
know.” In case of misconception we hasten to
state that this story isn’t true. AVe invented it
ourselves.
* * *
And so Melba is really coming to Aus-
tralia ! Here’s Williamson’s card up his sleeve,
though he is not exactly entrepreneur in chief.
And yet papa Dave Mitchell doesn’t look oyer-
pleased. He would prefer to cage this song bird
at Lilydale, or even at Yarraberg, where she used
to play the harmonium, and afterwards oblige at
church on Sundays.
* * *
How came English people to adopt the
absurd way of shaking hands now in vogue—with
the arm raised to a level with the shoulder?
Charlie Collette explains; “So many swells now
keep shops, and in shaking hands with their
friends across the counter they are obliged to raise
their arms in that way, and snobs who don’t keep
shops have imitated them.”
* • »
The toadying cablegrams so frequently
inflicted upon us about the English proceedings of a
certain rich Australian who affects British yacht-
ing society are understood to be engineered by
Somers ATne and Co. A T ine, it is remarked in
London, may yet be able to stop his friend from
eating peas with a knife, but he’ll never succeed
in bracing up his pal’s “ aitches.”
- e #
Nobody in Queensland accuses the Nor- r
man family of “ side.” The Governor spends his
spare time poking about the river in a canoe, or
strolling around the suburbs in an aimless kind of
way. Lady Norman braces herself up for the
Government ’Ouse ordeal by horse-exercise. They
patronise every local charitable punch-and-judy
show with heroic fortitude. Bometime ago the
Woollongabba “push” got up an entertainment
in aid of a local lame-dog asylum, or something of
that kind. The Governor and his family were
inveigled into being present. They turned up
unostentatiously and innocently, and placidly sat
the thing out. It was a “ rorty ” show too.
It’s getting quite a common tiling for tbe
youthful plaintiff in a seduction case to name the
most respectable hotel she can think of as the.
place where she and the defendant stayed as man
and wife. Then the licensee comes flaring to the
newspapers with a denial that any married couple
stayed at her hotel that night. And people smile
to see how the disclaimer gives itself away.

THE UNEMPLOYED PROCESSION.
(For The Bulletin.)
The melancholy line filed down the street —
Some hundreds, seedy, pale, their wistful eyes
Stared strangely at the well-dressed crowds who
saw
Them pass along, for it was evening ; soon
The actor, clown, and painted girl would play
To please the sneering groups who stood to watch.
And some in front had raised a marching song ;
I closed m-y ears, for ’twas a mockery.
But one stood near, a black, wond’ring, and asked
How ’twas the white men marched at such a time.
And when he heard that some must work or
starve,
He stared as unbelieving, and I thought
How awful that this savage—if he knew—
Might boast —“ Such piteous spectacle was not
Ere white man’s foot was here, for then when food
Was scarce wo starved together. ” And with
shame
I turned away, for all the lines had passed.
A. O. Fontaine.
Lane Cove.

The standard - bearers in those truly
mournful Melbourne unemployed processions are,
as often as not, respectably-dressed women, whose
genteel scruples have been silenced by the sobs of
their hungry children.
• « •
Lord llosebill, ex-aido to Gov. Hopetoun,
has come back to Australia with a in his
dressing-bag. He’s an earl now —the Earl of
Northesk. In appearance the young man is the
same strawberry-roan johnnie as of yore, and you
wouldn’t think ho had changed from a plain lord
into a “noble’’lord. But he is. If he were to
commit a felony the common or garden-law
couldn’t touch him —he could claim to be tried by
his peers. The point is worth remembering, not
in connection with this particular aristocrat, who
isn’t likely to commit a felony or anything else
that puts a strain upon the intellect, but as an in-
stance of the remarkable fact that the jury who
could find a man guilty whilst his father lived
couldn’t sit on the case if his noble progenitor
were dead.

shape of that hat is not more eminently respect-

down over his head and keeping it there. Mussy
wears the lofty tile assertively in private boxes at

Unless there be one law for the barristers
and another for clients in Adctoria it is evident
tiiat the genial Purves, Q.C., will have to ante-up
the seventy-thousand-pound baby-heiress, Kath-
leen Macpherson. His only shadow of an excuse
for seizing and sticking to the opulent infant lay
in the fact that her stepfather and legal guardian,
having been lured within the jurisdiction of the
Court, wasn’t domiciled within the blessed juris-
diction aforesaid. And considering that Purves
annexed the Golden Kid almost as soon as she had
beer! landed at Melbourne, there wasn't much
time allowed for the domiciling process anyway.
But now stepfather Barton is legally domiciled in
Melbourne, and intends to domicile there for ever-
more rather than let Purves have possession
of the little ward of Court. How the child came
to be made a ward, and what the dickens right
Purves has to interfere in the matter, are mys-
teries too deep for discussion in a paragraph,
Attorney-General Barton (of N.S.AV.) is much
more nearly related to the glittering Kathleen,
inasmuch as he is uncle to the child’s step father
ami great-uncle to her half-sister—an actual blood-
relation, in fact. Purves’ family interest in the
matter is a sort of Chinese puzzle. His wife’s
mother (when a widow) married the grandfather
of Kathleen when he was a widower, which means
that Kathleen is the grand-daughter of Purves’
wife’s stepfather. If the almighty Q.C. can claim
custody of the child on this evidence, he must
be entitled to assume control of every orphan
asylum in the country.
* * •
Tims a correspondent : .
Dear Bulletin,— He your recent paragraph concerning
wife-desertion in its relation to the theatrical profession.
People should be cautious about indiscriminately sym-
pathising with “ deserted ” women. Comparatively few
women who are of any account, or who are not to blame
themselves, are abandoned. Man is so much the creature
of habit, that it takes a good deal in the way of nagging,
drunkenness, or even infidelity, to drive him away from a
woman to whom he has become used. Then, again,
public performers nearly always get had or indifferent
women as wives. It may be almost granted that a woman
who is fascinated by a man because lie paints his face and
goes on the stage, is just as big a fool, and, morally as
worthless as the men who run mad after ballet-girls,
or the youths who make countesses of courtesans.
And, by the way, it is to he hoped that the next (Vic-
torian) judge who tries a divorce-case will not make such
an ass of himself as the last one did by his truly hysterical
and unworldly sympathy with the lady. His Honor said
“it was a pity that a woman separated from her husband
could not have a male friend without suspicion of
adultery.” If this he his Honor’s real opinion I don't
know what sort of notions they have in legal circles
regarding morality, hut the standard of morals in other
society is, no matter what people may say , utterly
different. A decent married woman separated from her
husband dare not have a male friend unless she wishes to
lose her reputation. It may be hard on her but it is one
of the punishments for marrying the wrong husband. It
is also hard on the prisoner in gaol that he can’t stroll
down to the Gardens and hear the hand play, or dine at
Paris House, hut that deprivation is one of the punish-
ments for his mistake whatever it may have been. That
Judge’s sillv remarks have no doubt made much mischief
among some already too frisky matrons. It would be
interesting to see how' the Judge referred to would act if
his own married sister went out to meet men at night
who kissed her on the brow and embraced her at parting ;
also, what he would say when she returned after a three
days’ absence with the vague information that she had
been away with “ a friend.” Understand me: 1 make no
imputation against the lady concerned—save what is
involved in the admitted facts of the case.—Yours, Badly
Bitten.

Dear Bulletin, — I have heard very, very
often that gibe about the women who haunt the
Australian divorce-courts when a particularly
thrilling case is in progress, and have almost to
be removed by violence before tney will quit.
What about the men who do exactly the same
thing, and are not invited to quit at all? If the
case is too offensive for women to listen to, lest
they should be morally infected, I presume men
are allowed to listen because they are so far gone
already that they can’t get infected any worse.
And if the average man is so morally “ high ” that
he is eminently fitted
to haunt the divorce-
court, isn’t he a nice
animal to criticise
woman for being
there also especi-
ally considering that
she isn’t there nearly
so often as he is ?
I would like to hear
some Australian
j udge invite all
gentlemen—including several dozen unattached
barristers—to leave the court when the objec-
tionable evidence is just starting, and then bear
how they would swear outside. —Yours, T. Salt.

Maoviluud just now is full of itinerant
titled potentates. Lord Massoreene, of AiUhrirn
beyant, is doing the Hot Lakes ; the Hon. Some-
thing Lowther, heir presumptive to the Earl of
Lonsdale, is doing ditto; and Sir Edward and Lady
Hash wood arc on a flying visit to the ladyships
pa and rna. Five years Dash wood
was a humble gumdigger at Kaipara. The death
of a relation suddenly converted him into a
baronet with 10,000 a year, and having married
the daughter of a village doctor on the Kaipara,
he gently laid down his gum-spear and spade and
put on Ids armorial bearings.
1 * # *
Wq passed by Hampden A ilia (Balmain)
the other evening, and. heird the most fearsome
row going on inside that wo ever listened to.
There would be upwards of two hundred dull
thuds, and then a sound as if forty cups and
saucers had been smashed at once, and after that
a series of fearful howls would .split the air, and
somebody could be heard smashing up the key-
board of a piano with an axe. Also a bloodshot
old man drove his white head right through the
front window and threw out a volume of poems
and two handfuls of hair into the front garden.
Then we knew what was the matter. It was
Parkes reading the cablegrams about Dibbs’
success in London, and making comments with a
tomahawk as he went along.
• * #
Parson Laseron, of Sydney, is to be tosti-
monialised with a purse of sovereigns because he
was shot in the back by Walker, M.L. A. The
average preacher can’t drop downstairs without
falling on a testimonial at the bottom. The logic
of tire affair isn’t very clear, however. People
pay to see a comedy because they enjoy it, arid
when they pay to see poor parson Laseron shot
it looks as if they enjoyed it too. If they put up
the money as an evidence that their faith in Mr.
Laseron is unimpaired and that they are sure lie
didn’t deserve to be shot, it is an insult—nobody
even hinted that he deserved to be shot. And if
they mean it as a sign of thankfulness that he
wasn’t shot any worse then they ought to give the
money to Walker, because he didn’t plug the
preacher in a vital-part.

The finding of the jury of four, who by a
three-fourths majority returned a verdict for
defendant in the Sydney breach-of-promise case,
Eitzallan versus Hunt, tried last week, plainly
did not meet with the approval of Judge Wiu-
deyer, who charged strongly in favour of plaintiff,
a rather pretty girl, who knows a thing or two.
For, when the jury returned to Court with the
verdict, after having been locked up some hours,
the Judge snapped out to them something to the
effect that they had been locked up at the instiga-
tion of defendant, who had not expected they
would give bin a verdict. Very likely not —after
such a cock-eyed summing-up. The jury’s
verdict, however little it may suit the deeply-
wronged plaintiff, who wanted .£2OOO as balm for
(sob) her outraged affections, is heartily approved
by the public.

The latest S.A. waterworks scheme, the
Happy Valley reservoir, is a monument of official
stupidity. The heart of the reservoir consists of a
decayed graveyard, and the works have been
stopped so as to remove the bodies.

When does aristocratic—or journalistic—
regard for the dead begin—or end? In the r.ews-
paper illustrations of Lord Ancram’s funeral at
Sydney the most “distinguished” ladies present
are depicted as standing within the palisading
surrounding a grave, and on the mound. Of
course the grave was that of some untitled
defunct.
* * *
To learn in how many different keys,
“lips glued to lips” can sharply ejaculate,
“Oh’s ! ’ and “ What’s that! ” you have only to
be in the neighbourhood of Mrs. Macquarie’s
Chair, Sydney Domain, when the nine-o’clock
gun is fired nightly from the flag-ship. If you
have sharp eyes, you can also discover in the
instant between the gun-fire and the following
bugle-blast, that 243 men, or thereabouts, have
only the one way of soothing the delicious
tremblings of the agitated little dear each holds
in his arms. There’s a lot of monotony about
masculine human nature when it comes to hugging,
somehow.

The father of the present Justice a’Beckett
and brother of the late Sir AV. a’Beekett, first
Chief Justice of Victoria, died in Melbourne last
week. He, Thomas Turner a’Beckett, son of
Gilbert a’Beckett, was a respectable old gentle-
man who had bis Australian path made smooth
for him in the early days, and enjoyed many
opportunities of serving his adopted country with
profit to himself. Also, lie claimed to be a distant
blood relation of his namesake, Thomas a’Beckett,
a prelate who met a violent death in the venerable
cathedral wherein Dr. Beaney was anxious to pur-
chase immortality.
Talking of Dr. Beaney, when are the horse
leeches going to suck at his estate under cover a,
an alleged doubt as to the validity of that Canter-
bury bequest ?

THAT IMMORTAL ASS.
The ass that once in Matthew’s halls
Could lay a cherished head,
Now parts for aye from Matthew's walls
A sold, forgotten Ned ;
For bust’s the boom of other days,
And Tory hill is o’er ;
The ass, a costly asset, brays
Beside a stranger's door.
No more for gaudy guests the Knight
Bids Neddy “give the paw,"
One bond alone still binds them tight—
The lesson in “ Hee-haw !”
The “patient creature’s ” less an ass
Than some who’vo dropped their gold,
Who, petted once, find now, alas !
That they've been left—and sold.

* # *
Oysters are dear just now. Therefore,
when you meet oysters on the half-shell at lunch
you may take long odds that the oyster-shells
were dug out of the rubbish-heap in the back-yard
where the cats fight at night, and carefully
washed, and then decorated with oysters out of a
tin. They look nice, but it’s a lot safer to stick to
the cold corned beef.

to trousers, as being “cleaner, healthier, and
warmer,” while easier to walk in and setting off
the figure better,
Dear Bulletin, —It was quite as easy,
don’t you think, to get money in Australia for the
Indian niggers at famine-time as it is to raise
contributions for our own starving unemployed ?
—Bell Metal.

A recently-established Southern church
has had a decided run of bad luck. One of the
lay-readers threw up the post and turned a
barkeeper, another was hauled up for obtaining
goods under false pretences, and the third de-
camped, leaving behind him many sorrowing
friends.

A fashionable German
physician named Wiederhold
is doing three months’ im-
prisonment for having be-
laboured a nervous lady
patient with a stick as she
lay in bed. The doctor de-
fended his course of treatment
on the ground that stick was
the only effective cure for
hysteria, a theory which
deserves the consideration of
all who have had any experi-
ence of lovely woman when the
spirit moves her to shut her
eyes and shriek.

Dear Bulletin, — When
I was in Darlinghurst Prison,
a few weeks ago, a prisoner
told me this melancholy yarn,
which I send you os only (of
course) a prisoner’s statement:
“I have now been in gaol
nearly four years for neglect
to maintain my wife. An
order was made against me by
a magistrate for maintenance ;
I neglected to comply with
it, was brought up, and sent,
to gaol for 12 months, or until
I did comply with it. The
rule is that, after 12 months,
a prisoner has a month’s
liberty in which he is supposed
to be able to earn sufficient
to enable him to comply with
the maintenence order ; if he
does not by that time pay
up arrears, he is sent up
again for a second 12 months,
and so on seriatim. But my
wife has struck a new line.
She has, at her elbow, a
lawyer “joker,” and directly
I leave prison, she swears an
information that I am “about
to leave the colony ” (N.S.W.),
and I am re - arrested and
shopped again. My fourth
year will be up in about 10
days, but, if I am not re-
arrested before I can pet to
the first pub. for a drink, I
shall be before I can swallow
sufficient to ‘ feel comfort-
able.’” Now, Mr. Bulletin,
in the words of the parodied
Shylock—“ Ish dot der law ? ”
—Yours, Ex-Prisoner.

In Stawell Cemetery (Vic.) over the grave
°f a youth is a wooden slab inscribed :
Grieve not for me, my parents dear,
I am above, and not here.
Then follows the additional touching legend :
He died of a fish-bone in his throat.

The South Melbourne Salvation Army
gives two free feeds daily to the paupers, except
on Sundays, when, by Providential dispensation,
there is no hunger, and they are filled by the big
drum, and wind from the euphonium.

capital punishment. Several persons have been
sentenced to death lately, but none of them have
been hanged.

even more persistent as a Government House
young man than the Chief Justice. He is a fat,
dumpy little fellow, but never misses a vice-regal
ball and invariably turns up on the railway plat-
form to meet or farewell travelling aristocrats.
One of his fads is to mount all the city troopers on
white horses. • ■

The Australian detective agency always
advertises ; “Debtors, Husbands, Lost Valuables,
tfec., found,” or something to that effect. Then
are several dozen of these offices for finding things
scattered throughout Australia, yet nobody evei
can find the thing he wants, any more than la
can lose the thing he doesn’t want. Why not
establish an office for losing things, which is, afte :
all, the greater want of the two ? Any man who
has ever tried to lose a poor relation, or a life
insurance canvasser, or a dead body, or a mother-
in-law will endorse this sentiment cordially.
* * *
The wife of a member of a certain club
lost her mother a little while ago, and put on such
deep trappings of woe that she looked like a dis-
consolate widow. Her husband says it is a
nuisance, for whenever ho meets a chum he is
saluted with the remark : “ Oh, here comes the
defunct !”

A ghastly threat is flourished before the
eyes of any Melbourne capitalist who disputes his
liability on burst-up bank or syndicate shares
which were not legally transferred when lie sold
them long ago. The poor wretch is politely told
to take his choice between paying up, or having
his case, if he win it in the first instance, taken
from court to court and right along to the Privy
Council, until the costs resulting from a possible
successful appeal would crush him, A man of any
means is the party to be squeezed these times, and
the winder-up who takes costs out of the blessed
estate, and can work the law without fear for him-
self personally, will squeeze without compunction.
Shell out or run the risk of having all your sub-
stance consumed by lawyers ! The poor wretch
aforesaid mostly feels disposed to utter one wild,
despairing cry and shell out.

The man who bought Deeming’s canary is
dead, the canary was eaten by the cat, and now
the cat is said to be pining away on the tiles. The
band of Fate is perceptible in all these matters.
Sheriff Anderson, who applied for Deeming’s body
at the door of the condemned cell, has been
forcibly retired from the public service because
he’s over GO, and several women who ogled Deem-
ing at the trial are likely to expire suddenly.
People begin to look anxiously at Judge Hodges
and Crown Prosecutor Walsh, Q.C., for the curse
of Deeming is upon them both, and they are cer-
tain to be sent for anon. There’s no getting away
from Fate. Everybody who had a hand in hang-
ing the rampageous plumber must go. His wives
and children, being directly responsible for his
doom, went first.
* * *
The female who threw half a brick, or
half a loaf, or something, at Gladstone whilst he
was addressing a Chester election meeting must be
one of the Duke of Westminster’s tenants. West-
minster is the Great Mogul of the stupid old city,
and since that pompous rent-collector put Glad-
stone’s portrait (by Millais) up to auction without
reserve and wiped his fat hands of all connection
with his former patron his tenants have been ex-
pected to curse ditto to the angry duke. Besides,
every Chester woman is a furious Tory from her
cradle upwards. Her ideas are hemmed in by an
ancient wall, and everything about her is as it was
in the middle ages. Gladstone will never do much
good at Chester until he turns up in chain armour
and doffs his plumed helm as he cries, “ The Queen
—God bless her ! ”

“ A Doctor’s Wife ” thus addresses The
Bulletin
Dear Bulletin, — There have always been wicked and
slanderous people who assert that in the private rooms
set apart for ladies at well-known city restaurants, the
refreshments imbibed are not strictly confined to
lemonade and tea. In fact, it is added, that these inno-
cent if cheerless beverages, are seldom called for. It is a
mystery known only to the initiated why a tiny tray of
coffee-bsans should find a place on all the small tallies.
The hour before noon is as good a time as any to call in
and take some mild refreshment in the privacy of “ the
ladies’ room.” The visitors then will be found to be of
the mixed types so favourable for observation. It must
be borne in mind that the matrons and maids who
drop in and indulge in “a peg” are of respectability
and good standing. No demi-mondaine would show
her face twice within the doorway. The thing
that most astonishes a newcomer is the calm way
in which girls, scarcely over 20, will toss off one,
and sometimes two, brandies-and-soda with ail the
equanimity of old hands. In point of fact, it is mostly
the young women who indulge in spirits, the elder ones
have their honest bottle of beer or stout ! The timid
novice, as a rule, asks hesitatingly for “ a glass of wine”
on her first visit, but she soon learns to take her nip like
a man, or rather, a woman. Still, for a long time, she
finds it necessary to explain to a friend that she has been
busy shopping, and that Dr. has told her that she
must take a little stimulant when she feels fatigued.
Sometimes there is a little shamefacedness visible when a
girl with a b.-and-s. before her recognises a friend whom
she is not particularly anxious to be seen by ; but mutual
explanations as to the cause of their requiring a little
refreshment soon smooth matters over, and they “ take
their glass ” sociably in company. Whisky is not, either
in Sydney or Melbourne, such a favourite drink as brandy
amongst the frequenters of the ladies’ room. Whether the
girls have taken their ideas from novels, written
by women, wherein the naughty characters all drink
brandy-and-soda, and sometimes, in moments of excite-
ment, half a tumbler of brandy neat, it is hard to say ;
but the fact remains that, given the same number of men,
whisky would almost invariably be the liquor in demand.
A favourite plan is to have the spirit brought in a hock-
glass, to make believe that it isn’t hie. How many of
these embryo topers develope into secret drinkers in
middle-age it would be hard to say. That the habit of
“nipping” is not going to be any longer monopolised by
men is evident at a glance ; and what is also evident is
the fact that most of them, young as they are, know how
to “ carry their liquor.” Occasionally a flushed face has
to be toned down by a dropped veil, and a voice ordinarily
“ soft and low ” begins to gallop at an unusual rate ; but,
as a rule, these—mostly upper-crust damsels—can swallow
their brandy-and-soda with as little effect on their outward
demeanour as their brothers would experience—perhaps
less. None of them reach the “jolly good fellow ” stage,
and no one, to meet them in the street, would imagine
that they had partaken of anything stronger than the
most innocent of drinks. There are some neat pen and-
ink pictures to be obtained in “the ladies’ room,” which
may be worth drawing some day. I have a neat two
columns in my mind’s eve.

Watch Your Nose!
Already the claim is made that one’s disposition
is outlined by his nose. According to the Buffalo
Commercial, large nostrils show courage. People
of fearless dispositions breathe fully and freely.
All the fiercer animals have dilated nostrils. The
drooping nostril shows histrionic talent. If the
nose also droops, it denotes a tragic power, and if
only the nostril the capacity is marked for the
interpretation of comedy. When the nose is thin
at the bridge it shows generosity, while a nose
that is thick at the bridge argues inquisitiveness.
When it is “ tip-tilted like the petal of a flower ”
the person is inquisitive. A projecting nose
argues a disposition to investigate. It is ahead of
the person as it were, and wants to scent out
things. Look at your nose in the glass and see
what sort of a person you are, any way. If your
nose is blue you have a bad circulation, if very
pale you are suffering from poverty of blood, if
full of blackheads, which you can squeeze out,
your blood is impure, if your nose has a rod color,
it may have cost you a lot of money to have got
up that beautiful brilliancy, and it may also cost
you a great deal still to keep it in repair; in either
case you are a victim of aggravated indigestion
caused by the liberties you take with your stomach
and liver, and must have immediate recourse to
that great regulator of all diseased conditions,
known as Clements Tonic, which, so far, has
never failed to cure a single case where it has been
given a fair chance. Follow the example of G.
Alexander Tather, Esq., 198 Myers-street, Gee-
long, Vic., who says : “It is a very pleasing duty
for me to convey to you the facts of the great
benefit that has accrued to me through taking
Clements Tonic. For the last two years I have
suffered very severely from indigestion, flatulence,
and I think every other horror appertaining there-
to, being subject to great uneasiness and retching
after meals, especially after dinner, which meal I
had to discontinue altogether, the nasty sensation
and retching depressed me so much. I tried
many of the advertised cures without benefit, but
on reading of a case (cured by Clements Tonic)
similar to my own, I resolved to give the same
remedy a trial, and, after taking three bottles, the
uneasy feeling and retching were entirely cured ;
and I shall not fail to make it known as widely as
possible in the Geelong district, where I reside,
and trust it may be the means of relieving others
so afflicted.”

A Victorian policeman unsuccessfully
appealed, the other day, against an order for the
maintenance of his illegitimate child. The judge
said there was a prima facie case of perjury on his
part, and made some apt remarks on the extra
value attached to a constable’s oath.
Never write a letter to a girl without first
considering how it will sound when a grinning
barrister reads it out in court. The following, pro-
duced in a recent breach of promise case, points a
moral: —
I could never take a secondary, washed-out course of
affection. I bring you a contrite heart. lam as pure as
a thistle-down. Oh, Jdne, Ido believe in the power of a
few to pour out their souls, and blessed are those few. It
is they, child, who live. The masses only exist. What
bliss in so doing ! What rapture 1 What celestial joy !
Attraction must be centred in a mother, and what means
a mother? A woman, a helpmate of man ; not a toy for
the hour, but his equal—but still a helpmate, a phantom
of delight, his second self, his companion, friend, adviser,
encourager, comforter, wife.
Brutal guffaws in court! Verdict for <£2so and
costs ! Furniture thrown at the defendant by his
bride when he got home !

WILLIAM JOHN LYNE.
“That Little Cold.”
At the present time the weather is most change-
able, and nearly everyone suffers from colds in the
hehd or throat. These affections in their primary
state are never very hurtful to the constitution,
but a neglected cold is one of the worst things
that can ever happen to a human being, It
should certainly bo attended to immediately.
Warmer clothing must be put on, and the patient
must undergo a slight course of a medicine which
will cure in the early stages and not allow the
sufferer to become afflicted with those most
prevalent complaints—ASTHMA, CONSUMP-
TION and BRONCHITIS.
What a number of persons have cause to regret
a neglected simple little cold, and if they had only
thought it was going to turn out as it has done
they would certainly have taken the PROPER
PRECAUTIONS. But still it is not too late,
and why? Because NATURE, which provides
everything necessary to existence, in her bounty
has given us herbs and vegetation amongst which
is hidden the antidotes to sickness, and it only
required the research of such a lady as MOTHER
AUBERT to discover the plants which yield the
articles of assimilation in curbing the ills brought
on by a slight cold.
“ Marupa ”
Is the registered name of MOTHER AUBERT’S
GOUGH. ASTHMA and CONSUMPTION
REMEDY.
It is a medicine which should be in every house-
hold, and testimony has been given by hundreds
of persons of its good effects. Notably MR.
WALTER BENTLEY and MISS MYRA
KEMBLE, whoso vocation brings them under
the notice of everybody “who runs and reads,’’
would not have certified to the efficacy of
“ Marupa ”
If it was not what it is made out to be.
There are no mineral substances in its compos!*
tion, and almost for that reason alone it is not
hurtful to the smallest child, but can be admin-
istered with perfect safety.
Mothers, attend to that distressing cough of
your little offspring and send for a bottle of
“ Marupa,”
And have cause to rejoice that in future years
your children grow up with CHEEKS LIKE
ROSES and not with PALLID LIPS because
“THAT LITTLE COLD” was neglected in
younger days.

Karana.
WHEN the digestive organs are out of order
a good reliable tonic is required to bring
them back to their normal state.
A number of persons upset the stomach by
neglecting to take their meals at regular stated
intervals, and others again eat to excess at some
particular meal and from these causes the
machinery gets out of order and does not run
smoothly. Late suppers, also, are one of the
causes of upsetting the digestive organs.
Sister Mary Joseph Aobert, in her researches
amongst the plants of New Zealand, has dis-
covered there is a very Valuable remedy for these
complaints in our own vegetation, and, after
experimenting for a number of years, has now
brought forward the medicine called
Karana.
Dr. Larochette, a chemist of the First Class
of the Faculty of Paris, to whom samples were
sent, says : —“ It is an excellent bitter tonic, and
I consider it even superior to the preparation of
Cinchona in all affections of the stomach. It has
given us, prepared as you sent it, the most satis-
factory results.” ■
The Reverend Sister says she has used it for
many years extensively and successfully with
sufferers of every age and constitution, and she
noticed that in a few cases it did not agree at
first* but by diminishing the dose good results
followed. She remarked astonishing good results
on old people, who had ruined their constitutions
by the abuse of intoxicating drink, and who were
debilitated and suffering after having turned over

HE DID'NT ANTSCIPATE
TRORBLE
AN amorous couple on the verge of committing
matrimony entered a Paddington ’bus the
other night, and oblivious of the presence of a
third passenger, who to all appearances was enjoy-
ing a comfortable nap in the top corner, com-
menced billing and cooing in dead earnest. Of
course the conversation turned upon the subject
of their approaching nuptials, and many were the
expressed anticipations of the joy that awaited
them in that estate which all are anxious to enter
into and not a few would be glad to obtain release
from. “All,” whispered the fair one, suppressing
a mild hysterical sob, “ what a pleasure it will be
for me to listen to all your troubles and share all
your griefs.” ‘ But, darling,” rejoined the hope-
ful Romeo, “ I have no griefs nor troubles.”
“Ob, but you will have plenty, you know, when
wo arc married,” added she, with ingenuous
emphasis. The passenger in the corner coughed,
and a silence followed that would have tipped
down the scale at £OO pounds. The prospective
benedict assumed a thoughtful mood. Up to that
moment he had regarded marriage as an unmixed
blessing—a path strewn with roses—not even once
pausing to think of the discomfort attending a
pair of feminine cold feet, of an icy reception
after a late night at the lodge or club, of a colicky
baby with leathern lungs and a voice like the
shrillest of steam whistles, or of bruised and
lacerated fingers, the combined result of unskilful-
ness, a blunt tomahawk, and a chunk of knotty
firewood. These, however, are among the little
troubles that the average young man undertaking
one of the great responsibilities of life must look
out for, and the best thing for him to do to
soothe the self-inflicted injuries of inexperience,
and to prevent the attack of chill or rheumatism
which shooting by night at lightning speed out of
a warm bed, and rushing wildly along linoleum-
covered passages in quest of paregoric will inevit-
ably bring, is always to have in the house ready
for use a supply of St. Jacobs Oil, the great con-
queror of all pain. Mr. John Breakwell, of
Milson’s Point-road, North Sydney, gratefully
acknowledges ; “I have suffered severely for some
years from rheumatism in my knees, and have
tried many remedies. I have found St. Jacobs
Oil give me great relief, and am pleased to speak
in praise of it.”

I had been troubled five months
with Dyspepsia. The doctors told
me it was chronic. I had a fullness
after eating and a heavy load in the
pit of my stomach. I suffered fre-
quently from a Water Brash of clear
matter. Sometimes a deathly Sick-
ness at the Stomach would overtake
me. Then again I would have the
terrible pains of Wind Colic. At
such times I would try to belch and
could not. . I was working then for
Thomas McHenry, Druggist, Cor.
Irwin and Western Ave., Allegheny
City, Pa., in whose employ I had
been for seven years. Finally I used
August Flower, and after using just
one bottle for two weeks, was en-
tirely relieved of all the trouble. I
can now eat things I dared not touch
before. I would like to refer you to
Mr. McHenry, for whom I worked,
who knows all about my condition,
and from whom I bought the medi-
cine. I live with my wife and family
at 39 James St., Allegheny City, Pa.
Signed, John D. Cox. @

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Grand National meeting opens at 11cm-
jnjjlon, on Saturday', and a mild, half-hearied desire to
“pickup the double” is manifesting itself amongst a
community that would bo very glad to pick up a six-
pence with a hole in it just at present. The betting-
mark J, I bus far, has supplied no valuable information con-
cerning the big hurdle race, and if I lie information given
al the finish is no more valuable than it was last year,
win nail the favouritis came in at the wrong end covered
with more mud than glory, it won’t be worth having,any-
way. The Millers have the gigantic Redleap and
Norbert, either of which might win—especially the latter,
because it is so generally stated that the horse
jo-gn’t jump kindly. A horse that doesn’t jump
well on paper is frequently a perfect devil to fly the
sticks in his stride. The Pioneer, a common-looking
animal and bad performer on the flat, is ostensibly
“fancied” by Sam Cooke, also Ilavilah, from Scobie’s
stable, at Ballarat, presents the hazy appearance cf “a
rod in pickle.” Properly backed at the post, Ilavilah
ought to finish near the front indeed—if he doesn’t fall
ami break Ills neck in the meantime. An outside chance,
Abergyle, jumps well, and he might win ; hut, taking one
consideration with another, The Bulletin inclines to the
opinion that “ the field ” is a good horse which generally
just ill-s the confidence of the hook-nosed Hebrew who
backs it.
# # #
On Tuesday, July 12th, a race-meeting for the
benefit of the Melbourne unemployed will be held at
Caulfield. All the officials have volunteered their ser-
vices gratis, the horses are determined to run straight for
this occasion only, and the free-list will, it is hoped, be
suspended by general consent. Even Lord Hopetoun and
suite have announced their gracious desire to pay for
admission, just as though they were ordinary mortals, so
perhaps the newspaper proprietors will forego the usual
charge for advertisements and purchase tickets for their
sporting representatives. We say, “perhaps.’*
* * *
Strathmore, on last year’s running in the same
race and subsequently, is the best handicapped of all the
t qi weights for the Melbourne Cup, although the public
will probably take Zalinski for choice it the two stable
companions come fit to the post—which is not very likely
1 1 happen. Another attractive article is Malolo, a second
edition of his sire (Malua) in appearance, and weighted at
two stone less than Strathmore and Malvolio. The Mel-
bourne Cup is yet a long way off, however. This time
l,st year people were talking of Carbine, Vengeance,
Dreadnought, and Melos, and not one of the four gave
the punters a run for their money.
# * »
The Cup conditions as regards three-year-olds
need amending. Experience shows that the weight-for-
age scale in the spring, over two miles, is not equitable
as concerns the horses of that age. Look at the anomaly,
too, of a crack two-year-old being weighted at Sst. in the
Cup, when there is every chance of his defeat in the
Derby, the winner of which, most likely the best colt of
the year, is only asked to carry 7st. 61b. It happened in
Titan’s case, and will happen again. The scale ought to
slide down if it ho just that it slide up.
# # *
Noah Beal, the oldest trainer in Australasia,
has a couple of Cup candidates in Forty Winks and
Penance—a possible two-Cup double. Penance, 7st. 711*.,
is supposed to be reserved for the big event. He once
gave Slromlioli a stone and a beating, and ran Carbine a
great race, weight-for-age. Forty Winks was well up last
Melbourne Cup, third in Australian and Sydney Cups,
and beat a good field in the Autumn Handicap, carrying
lib. less than he has in Caulfield Cup.
* * *
The A. J.C. has called upon the Surprise party
(Surprise is a c wintry horse, of course) for an explanation
of their running at Moorefield the other day. There are
two men on the committee who will surely not have the
“ neck ” to adjudicate in this case.
• # *
Re Team, owner of Malvolio, is dimly sus-
pected of having hacked his big moke for the next Cup a
fortnight before the weights were due. The hookies had
the cast-iron gdl to offer an extravagant 20 to I against
Malua’s son 10 days ago !
* * *
About once in every three months, more or less,
Skinner, a Melbourne hotel-keeper and racecourse refresh-
ment caterer, is robbed of a big bag of money. The
remorseless eye of the football barrackor and sporting
jnafer is ever on Skiimerand his hags of money, and as an
object of plunder he grow s monotonous.
* * *
The lucky Richmond (Vie.) baker who scooped
“Tatt.’s ” last Melh. Cup sweep w ith Malvolio, is laying
out the money building villas at Brighton.

The president of a backblocks racing club had a
horse in the Selling Race, “and put him in the bag,” a
bookie standing in. One of the stewards hearing this
went to the three other jockeys and promised them a
fiver each to lose. Then he got a caution administered to
the rider of the stiff. The latter had to win, and owner
and bookmaker lost heavily'.

The Wanted Song.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
fling us a song of songs—
Singer of things to be-
gad with a thousand wrongs,
Strong as the surging sea.
AA'ild as a wind (lint sweeps
Out of the dappled dawn,
Over a world that sleeps,
Dreaming of life new born.
Fling us a song afloat
Up to the skies above,
Passion in every note—
Human with burning love—
Brotherly love, and oh,
Waken the theme anew,
Sung in the long ago
Under the Syrian blue.
So as the winds and tide
Waken the slumh’ring main,
Here in the light denied
Life may awake again.
This is the hour of time,
This is the day of days ;
Sing us a song sublime—
Sing us a Marseillaise !

Despite the fluctuations in silver, it still
takes 6d. to buy a fair drink of decent whisky.
How is it that restaurateurs always have
an instinct as to what their rivals put on the table?
Same way with the rival newspapers in any town,
slavishly copying each other’s make-up.
To extract the fact of an anti-labour state-
ment from a capitalistic daily, divide by 50; or
multiply an admission in favour of labour by a
like amount. In either case the result will be
three parts capitalist lie and one part labour truth.

did I advocate land-settlement that often at Mass
I laid aside the Testament, and read the proclam-
ations in the Government Gazette announcing
where land had been thrown open for selection.”

The big Frankston (Vic.) Cemetery, which
is as yet a gruesome dream of the future, will
perhaps see the last of earth-to-earth funerals
from the Melbourne district. A piece of country
12 miles in circumference has been pegged out
there for “Christian” burials, anyway, and
although the rapid filling up of metropolitan
cemeteries must eventually drive all the trade to
Frankston, the demand for freehold allotments
will so diminish that the whole 12-mile area may
never be taken up. Cremation is in the air, and
a gigantic bakehouse for the benefit of right think-
ing corpses is already talked about. The manager
of St. Kilda grave ground has brought a charming
little model of a crematorium, fitted with all the
latest improvements, under the august notice of
his Board, and the Board saw that it was
good. Several of the members decided to be
burned on the spot, or rather they decided
on the spot that they would like flames, in-
stead of worms, to consume their cadavers.
Two or three Victorian Judges have hinted
an intention to go straight out for cremation in
their wills, the model aforementioned having
touched their hearts. To understand the working
of the new system is to become its advocate,
unless, indeed, you hug' the belief that worms
never attack dead ’unsof good position, or, again,
unless you cherish the notion that the immortal
soul is a kind of spiritual sweetbread all traces of
which might get lost amongst the ashes in the
oven. The days of the heavy polished-oak coffin
are numbered (for the crematorium body-gear is
necessarily of the lightest wood), and the dying
poet who talks of sleeping with the daisies on his
breast will soon be talking an anachronism.
Use Batho’s Baking Powder.
Some years ago Jack S , resident in a
large N.S.W. town, a notorious practical joker
and a man of great shrewdness and natural ability,
having become insolvent, had to appear in person
before the examining Judge in Insolvency. When
his name was called, Jack wandered sheepishly
around the court and had to be placed in position
by an official to whom, with an air of mock sim-
plicity, he said, “ Thank you, your worship.”
Jack pretended he did not see the Judge, and
when that functionary asked him what explana-
tion he had to make of his bankruptcy he turned
hastily round and replied with a startled look,
“ I’m guilty, your Honor, I plead guilty.” The
Judge proceeded to inform him that he was not
called upon to plead at all, when he broke in with
great earnestness, “ I’m guilty, your Honor, I
know I’m guilty.” The Judge here peremptorily
demanded a detailed explanation of the causes
leading to his insolvency, but failed to elicit any-
thing from the examinee but “ I’m guilty, your
Honor, I plead guilty.” A threat to commit him
for contempt did not shake Jack, who meekly
responded, “Yes, your Honor, I’m guilty.” At
last the Judge completely lost patience and said
angrily, “ Why, this man is an absolute fool, and
people who trusted him in business matters deserve
to lose their money.” Certificate granted. Where-
upon Jack again chipped in with, “ I’m guilty, your
Honor. I’ll come up there alongside you and tell
you all about it”—making a feint of getting up
beside the Judge, who immediately ordered his
removal from the court. Outside Jack met an
insolvent acquaintance whose case happened to
be next on the list. “ How did you get on,” asked
he of the joker. “ Oh, great! ” said Jack, “ I
just pleaded guilty to everything the Judge said,
and kept on pleading and he gave me my ticket
straightaway. You try it.” “I will,” said Jack’s
friend, who happened to be as simple as Jack was
deep, and who when brought before the court
followed Jack’s instructions exactly and began to
plead guilty at every turn. “ Were you present
at the previous examination ?” said the Judge.
The insolvent, instead of answering directly,
merely ejaculated, “I’m guilty, your Honor.”
The Judge now appeared to suspect that S
was not so foolish as ho pretended, and that the
insolvent under examination had been led the
success of S ’s strategy into following his
example. So he suspended his certificate for six
months, and informed him that he felt very much
inclined to commit him for contempt of court.

number of two-fifths of the whole. Considering

profits of the Melbourne and Sydney Mr. Moulds?

tipstering with his very miscellaneous literaria by

It might not be difficult for many readeis
to recognise a portrait of the old Melbourner we
will call Goole, who lived on Masonic dinners.
Poor as a rat, he preserved his dress-suit, as an in-
dispensable tool of trade, well saved, but not a
world too wide for his shrunken stomach, because it
never shrunk. He sniffed out evei’y dinner of the
mystic tie. As regularly as Banquo at Macbeth’s
feast, he was there. Brethren said, like Macbeth,
“ We only miss our dear brother Goole ’’—and his
Ally Sloper nose came round the edge of the door.
Nothing in modern times has been so copied as
Wolfe’s Schnapps. Beware of imitations.
Writes a London correspondent:—The
day after the Australian mail arrives in London
numberless Bulletin paragraphs are annexed and
published in the evening papers without the
slightest acknowledgement. Many scribblers thus
obtain a name as authorities on Australian affairs.
You must not altogether blame the editors, for
these pars, are re-written and palmed off as
original, and a hard-worked man cannot read
every paper to know where the “crib” comes
from. These literary thieves remind me always of
the story of the late Lord Lamington (or his
father, 1 forget which). Somebody asked him
where the celebrated monastery La Trappe was,
to which he replied that he had not the faintest
idea. His friend then commenced to quote a long
account of his Lordship’s visit to what he had
designated the living tomb ; how one saw the
brothers passing each other without exchanging a
word, whilst in some part of the grounds one^ of
them might be seen digging his own grave. The
noble Lord could only say : “ I assure you I have
never been there in my life.” “Do you mean to
say,” asked his questioner, “that you did not
write that article which is published in your
name?” “Oh, by the way,” said he, “now I
remember, I did, but I never was at the place. I
wrote it from something I saw in Galignani.”
Which was anticipating a good deal of modern
journalism.
