The patron saint of Griffithland St.
Helena.
Stead is coming out with a half-penny
paper—which will make the third in the field in
London.
An actor recently divorced at the instance
of his wife was one of the jury in the Mount
Rennie case.
A Melbourne woman named Cox has just
died leaving a quarter of a million’s worth of
public-houses.
The Sydney Hospital authorities are
“privately” investigating a big scandal in con-
nection with their medical staff.
The farmer-like, easy-going Marquis of
Drogheda, who visited Australia a year or two
ago, is dead. He was a harmless and unnecessary
old gentleman.
The associated Australian daily newspaper
proprietors are working all they know in the
direction of supplanting compositors by type-
setting machines.
In N.S.W. there are now, remarks G. H.
Reid, 28,450 freeholders possessing about 30,000,000
acres, but half of that vast area is held by about
73 individuals only.
Speaking at Cobar railway opening,
Minister Lyne (N.S.W.) said that Victoria had
raised on the broad shoulders and financial ability
of N.S.W. Premier Dibbs a loaivof two millions.
London County Council is inserting clauses
in all its contracts compelling contractors to pay
the Union rates of wages in London. The Mel-
bourne Board of Works doesn’t, however, “see its
way ” yet!
In Sydney Morning Herald last week,
“a Secretary for Political Association,” was
advertised for. “Must be good platform speaker
and of good social position.” Evidently a Tory
gang. National Ass. ?
Minister of Works Graham promises to
find work for the leaders of the Melbourne un-
employed. “I’ll find those agitators a job—in
Pentridge.” But he dare not. Meanwhile, how-
ever, uniform and plain-clothes police elbow their
way about among the hundreds of men at the
Government Bureau,
W. K. Thomson’s mansion at North
Brighton, the cost of decorating the interior of
which was close on £5OOO, still lacks a tenant.
The bank holds it per a professional gardener.
At first £30,000 was asked, but now it is said
£20,000 will get it. A Broken Billionaire
negotiated for the place at a figure mid-way
between these amounts, and would have got it
but for his wife’s objection; “Dear me! one can
actually see into the backyards of those houses
over there.” And so it was, for across a big
paddock several villas face the mansion rearwards.
One of Mr. Thomson’s married daughters runs a
florist’s shop in the centre block of Collins-street,
To Sir Mat. Davies’ sale last week female
Toorakia tumbled up bravely. Go to whichever
room one would, there sat rows of solemn, fat
duchesses. They did not come to buy the
Hebrew did all that. When the auctioneer an-
nounced “Tea!” the kitchen was stormed. The
house was but half-furnished, for it seemed as if
only the heavier articles had elected to face the
deluge after good Mat.’s departure. It was found
necessary to post placards all over the grounds,
requesting visitors to leave the plants alone, but,
in spite of these, daphne and other choice plants
were freely levied on for cuttings.
Young J. B. Watson, son of the Bendigo
millionaire, is dealing out the dollars more freely
for charitable purposes than did bis late father.
The worn-out miners’ sustentation Lind, which he
handsomely endowed, is one of his ideas.
Sir Bryan O'Loghlen, when speaking at a
“Home Rule” meeting in Melbourne last week,
asked his audience if Victoria would not rather be
governed badly by herself than well governed by
N.S. Wales, Tasmania, &c. Hurroo!
Nymphs of the pavement are now as bold
and as numerous in Melbourne as at the time of
the 18S0 Exhibition. A consignment of about 20
arrived from Germany a few weeks since, and it
is said that more are coming.
Rumoured that the cayenne in the first
course of the menu of next N.S.W. Parliamentary
session will be the submission of three motions for
the expulsion of three members who have been
prominently flagstaffed lately.
Dibbs, it is said, is to be rc-offered the
anti-republican bribe of a title. Should he accept
it he will have turned his back on the Republican-
ism he professes. Titles are the brands of the
enemies of the Australian nation.
The estate of “ Shane’s Park ” Harris, of
Sydney, who died intestate, gives .£13,000 each
to thirty of Ids relatives. Had it been realised
during the land-boom the properties would have
realised a million instead of £lOO,OOO.
Hennikcr Heaton has ' been returned
for Canterbury unopposed. And the Congress of
the Chambers of Commerce has delighted his
heart by passing, with only two dissentients, a
resolution in favour of his Empire penny-post.
A mass of medical and other evidence has
now come to hand to prove that Deeming was an
epileptic and inferentially irresponsible. How-
ever, they were in a hurry to hang Deeming, and
he was hanged accordingly. Justice is eternal,
and no sentence is worth the paper it is written on
that will not stand the test of six months’ con-
sideration.
Able-bodied seamen’s wages at Hobart
(Tas.) have been reduced from £5 a month to
£3 10s. ditto. InNewcastle (N.S.W.) the miners
have had their wages lowered sadly, but some of
the mine-owners still grab 20 per cent, dividend.
In fact, the wages are lowered in order to preserve
the shareholders’ kidney-fat. The same old pitiful
refrain runs in all these plaints—“ The men
refused to accept the reduction, but their places
were soon filled.”
After all lias been said, the chief crime
committed by George Black, M.L.A., plaintiff in
the libel-action, Black v. Norton, consists in his
having for so many years, alike in prosperity and
adversity, stood by the woman after taking the
false step—just as the chief crime committed by a
more eminent politician consists in having married
his mistress instead of “casting away his sin,”
i.e., abandoning her to her own resources. Black,
however, practically lost his case on account
of the savage and blistering vituperation impru-
dently launched by him at Norton in response to
the latter’s attacks, and of the adroitness with
which the jury were prejudiced against him for
his Anti-Royalist, Republican, and Agnostic
utterances.
The real reason of the chronic hostility
displayed by the British Press whenever a new
loan is nowadays.proposed by this country lies in
the fact that dfar increased expenditure is not
sufficiently tended to keep the British manufac-
turing exporter to the front.
Against the Labour movement in Queens-
land the united forces of Parliament, pulpit and
press are closing in. No one outside knows how
bitter the struggle is—the hatred and oppression
of one side, and the silent, sullen endurance of the
other. It looks, sometimes, as though Queensland
will yet be the Labour Marathon.
In a Brisbane [daily a Northern planter
wails his dread that the new regulations may
prevent sick Islanders and women “not in the
best of health ” from sewing up the sugar in bags.
Ugh ! Consumption ; syphilis ; elephantiasis !
You should sec how Kanaka sugar is made.
G. H. Reid, who announced on behalf of
N.S.W. Opposition an anti-Dibbs crusade which
is never to cease until the Ministry are forced to
the country, was very severe on Attorney-General
Barton when the latter unfolded the Ministerial
policy ip “a hole-and-corner way ” at the little
village of Manly, instead of before his East
Sydney constituents. Now, Mr. Reid (who also
represents East Sydney) instead of speaking there
hies himself to the dead town of Liverpool, most
of the inhabitants of which are in the local
Benevolent Asylum.
The farmer-in-town, when perfectly sober,
is generally a joyless animal, but if he will only
visit the splendid agricultural implement estab-
lishment of Messrs. Howard, Pitt-street, Sydney,
he will have untold comfort administered to his
bush-hardened, sun-baked soul. What strikes the
visitor as he wends his way through the fine show-
room with the first-rate assortment of ploughs,
harrows, and general agricultural implements
neatly arranged on either side, all fitted out with
the latest and best improvements, is the wonderful
progress that this firm has made—on the principle,
no doubt, that worth must tell. Steel-binders,
improved mowers, horse-rakes, chaff-cutters, Dix
harrows, Clayton-Shuttleworth portable engines,
and ploughs adapted to different kinds of soil, are
all there, and the experienced eye of the farmer
sees in them visions of coming wealth. At ene
end of the offices, with their beautiful American
walnut fittings, may be seen Mr. J. C. Howard,
engaged in his extensive correspondence; Mr.
Chas. Howard is everywhere, giving to customers
the benefit of his practical knowledge in their
purchases. Visitors may reckon on a courteous
reception from Manager Costie.
The public is an ass. Another strike at
Broken Hill, owners and miners quarrelling,
never thinking that the community pays the piper
in the long run. Strikes are barbarous. Arbitra-
tion spells civilization and the public, the third
and principal party to all conflicts, should exercise
its power and say strikes shall not be. Likewise
the man who wants protection without ruination
should keep valuable documents, jewellery and
cash in the Sydney Safe Deposit.*

They Met at the Morgue.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“On Thursday last there met at the Morgue an old
couple of apparently 60 years of age who separated by
mutual consent 40 years ago, the day after landing from
England, the wife going into service and he taking up a
piece of country land. Hearing of her illness he started
to see her, but died in the train and was removed to the
Morgue, where his wife had been taken the day before.”—
Melbourne Evening Paper.
“Now,” my friend said, “if you have finished
lunch I will show you a slice of real tragedy.” So
we made for the Morgue and in 10 minutes were
flattening our noses against the
Screen of glass we’re thankful for.
On the other side the six slate slabs supported
their loads of wrecked humanity—the flotsam and
jetsam of life’s boisterousness. A block of wood
beneath the head of each played the part of
pillow, and the dripping Yan Yean did duty for
tears.
At the far end lay the dead body of a child
which had lived but six hours ; a piece of tape tied
tightly about its throat told the story of a mother’s
anguish, and the child’s death, and on the slab
next lay the mother herself. “ I should not have
known they were mother and child, sir,” the
attendant said, “but for the two pieces of tape
being alike ; then it all came out, and I put them
next each other.” On the adjoining slabs, and
close together, lay the objects of our mission—an
old couple, withered and life-weary, with faces
across which Trouble had engraved his sign-
manual too deeply for death to erase. They were
both over sixty, and, I thought, singularly alike.
“Years and years ago,” my friend said, “that
woman there—toothless and furrow-browed—was
the beauty of my father’s parish, admired and
flirted with by every man, from the squire down.
I) cantering to the meet, would stop at her
mother’s cottage, and beg a flower from her, and the
parson himself had been seen to chuck her under
the chin. Molly was an institution—somehow it
became neither sin nor scandal to love her. She
took butter round and left at each house smiles
and a sense of gladness with it. Don’t look at
her !—time belies everything—nor at me, and I’ll
•ontinue.
“ Molly gave me flowers until she got my heart
by way of payment, and then, as kittens do with
babies’toys, she played havoc with it—the wild,
wayward child of natu.ro rolled about with me on
the mossy banks of her own woods, pelted me
with primroses and buried me in blue bells until,
without her, life became grey and purposeless—
nor there, in the sanctity of Nature’s self,' had
modern moralities hold of us. Each to the other
grew transparent simultaneously, and we were all
natural and quite holy in the free forest, where
weeds could not grow nor sin exist —in that it was
mi manured of club gossip or women’s curiosity.
I think when I look back at those days, and
rein ember how the boisterous ecstasy of our mere
iving would now quiet down into the golden smt-
s «t of an hour of love and confidence—only to live
?p vin and awaken the woods with laughter—that
ne very gods must have envied and instanced us
ev ffl e,Ki e of life’s possibilities. But even Molly’s
joods were not sacred from the Goddess of Ven-
geance, who one night led my father there. We,
rouchmg together beneath the bushes, caught
ne sound of mumbling in the distance —it grew
s ? er a °d nearer until we heard the words tnem-
ves. It was my father preparing the morrow’s
i-nnon. He was practising his peroration, and
the sentences have burnt themselves into my
memory; “And the everlasting decree of nature
that has echoed down through the ages is, ‘ There
is no peace, saith my God for the wicked.’ ” And
then he was upon us, and stood over us in the
moonlight—and grew old. Molly had jumped to
her feet and stood with her back to him, motion-
less and statuesque. I bent my head only and
felt afraid, while he, my good father, took the
truth down with an audible gulp, turned on his
heels and left us without a word.
“ The next morning my father sent for me, and
there in his study and in the stately verbosity of
an English country parson, he sentenced me to a
year’s transportation to Germany—where I was
to forget Molly and regain my social status.
“ I came back at the end of the year to find that
my people, for my sake, had induced Molly to
marry the groom and emigrate with him to
Australia.
“ Harry, the groom, was the last man for Molly
to marry. My father had endeavoured to mix
incompatibles—Harry’s ferret-loving, dog-train-
ing, stable-bred autocracy with thenature-endowed
gentility of this‘queen of the rosebud garden of
girls.’
“The result was that on the voyage out they
quarrelled irrevocably. The girl, in her last and
only letter to me, said : ‘ Harry triesso hard to be
good to me, but I cannot live with him. I cannot
love him, 1 don’t know why, unless it is because I
cannot forget you—the old wood at home—and
our evenings there.’ And so, forty years ago,
when they landed here, they parted. Finally, he
went to the bush and took up a small holding,
and she went into service.
“‘And the years indignant worked their will.’
Molly faded away into a machine for the cooking
of chops, her old beauty died out, the sourness of
life and its disappointments converted her from
the wayward wildflower I knew her into a lemon
born out of due season, and he moped himself
into a misanthrope—dug potatoes, felled timber,
and cursed bullocks till the district and its chil-
dren looked upon him as a thing to be avoided.
Six weeks ago Molly broke a blood-vessel and
her mistress, in return for years of slavery, carted
her to the Melbourne Hospital—and there she
would have died had not a kindly visitor
removed her into lodgings and advertised
for her husband, whom she had seen but
four times since her arrival in Port Phillip.
For a great longing had seized the shrivelled
heart of her to see Harry once more and “ make
it up,” and he on his death-bed in his cottage in
Gippsland read the advertisement, and putting on
his old, threadbare black coat started for Mel-
bourne.
On the day he left his home, Molly died, and
was that afternoon brought here, and he, when he
heard of it, died also, and was given the next slab,
and so they met, you see, after all.
MANNINGTON CAFFYN.
The Salvation Army meeting is the Italian
opera of the musically-uneducated.
A large piece of petrified pork has been
found in a Maorlland river, and sent over to N.S.
Wales as a curiosity. Considering that you can
find petrified soup at almost every Sydney board-
ing-house, and beef which is so petrified that you
are liable to be brained with the gravy, we don’t
see where the curiosity comes in.
Wolfe's Schnapps is the greatest tonic and
most beneficial stimulant of the dav.
Borderland.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I am back from up the country—very sorry that I
went
Seeking for the Southern poets’ land whereon to
pitch my tent;
I have lost a lot of idols, which were broken on
the track —
Burnt a lot of fancy verses, and I’m glad that I
am back.
Further out may be the pleasant scenes of which
our poets boast,
But I think the country’s rather more inviting
round the coast—
Anyway, I’ll stay at present at a boarding-house
in town
Drinking beer and lemon-squashes, taking baths
and cooling down.
Sunny plains ! Great Scot! —those burning wastes
of barren soil and sand
With their everlasting fences stretching out across
the land !
Desolation where the crow is ! Desert ! where
the eagle flies,
Paddocks where the luny bullock starts and stares
with redden’d eyes ;
Where, in clouds of dust enveloped, roasted
bullock-drivers creep
Slowly past the sun-dried shepherd dragged
behind his crawling sheep.
Stunted “peak” of granite gleaming, glaring!
like a molten mass
Turned, from some infernal furnace, on a plain
devoid of grass.
Miles and miles of thirsty gutters—strings of
muddy waterholes
In the place of “shining rivers ” (walled by cliffs
and forest boles).
“ Range !” of ridges, gullies, ridges, barren ! where
the madden’d flies—
Fiercer than the plagues of Egypt—swarm about
your blighted eyes !
Bush! where there is no horizon! where the
buried bush man sees
Nothing. Nothing! but the madding sameness of
the stunted trees !
Lonely hut where drought’s eternal—suffocating
atmosphere—
Where the God forgotten hatter dreams of city-
life and beer.
Treacherous tracks that trap the stranger, endless
roads that gleam and glare,
Dark and evil-looking gullies—hiding secrets here
and there!
Dull, dumb flats and stony “rises,” where the
bullocks sweat and bake,
And the sinister “gohanna,” and the lizard, and
the snake.
Land of day and night—no morning freshness,
and no afternoon,
For the great, white sun in rising brings with him
the heat of noon.
Dismal country for the exile, when the shades
begin to fall
From the sad, heart-breaking sunset, to the new-
chum, worst of all.
Dreary land in rainy weather, with the endless
clouds that drift
O’er the bushman like a blanket that the Lord
will never lift—
Dismal land when it is raining—growl of floods
and oh ! the “ woosh ”
f the rain and wind together on the dark bed of
the bush —
Ghastly fires in lonely humpies where the granite
rocks are pil’d
In the rain-swept wildernesses that are wildest of
the wild.
Land where gaunt and haggard women live alone
and work like men,
Till their husbands, gone a-droving, will return to
them again—
Homes of men ! if homes had ever such a God-
forgotten place,
Where the wild selector’s children fly before a
stranger’s face.
Horae of tragedy applauded by the dingoes’ dismal
yell,
Heaven of the shanty-keeper —fitting fiend for
such a hell —
And the wallaroos and wombats, and, of course,
the “ curlew’s call ”
And the lone sundowner tramping ever onward
thro’ it all!
I am back from up the country—up the country
where I went
Seeking for the Southern poets’ land whereon to
pitch my tent;
I have left a lot of broken idols out along the
track,
Burnt a lot of fancy verses—and I’m glad that I
am back —
I believe the Southern poet’s dream will not be
realised
Till the plains are irrigated and the land is
humanised.
I intend to stay at present—as I said before—in
town
Drinking beer and lemon-squashes—taking baths
and cooling down.
HENRY LAWSON.
Necessity is the father of the half-caste.—
Kanaka proverb.
The world’s production of silver has in-
creased yearly, without interruption from
03,000,000 ounces in 1873 to 141,000,000 ounces in
1801, which represents somewhere about 125 per
cent, in 18 years. The United States output,
which has been augmented by some 115 per cent.,
will probably diminish shortly unless the Bimetal-
lists carry the day in the coming elections. By
getting leading producers to believe that a lucra-
tive market would be found for all the silver they
could raise abandoned mines were reworked with
renewed energy. Now that matters look dark for
the free coinage party, silver is quietly rising in
price. It is, however, highly improbable that the
metal will ever see a quotation of 4s. an ounce
again.
A pretty but very sleepy little town in
Western Victoria is mostly owned by one man
who resides at the local hotel. When any
affluent-looking visitors reach that town this
potentate generally offers to show them all the
objects of interest in the vicinity. Then he takes
them up a hill, and solemnly points to three
houses in different parts of the landscape. “That
’ouse over there,” he says, “ is the ’ouse where Hi
was born. That ’ouse on the left is the ’ouse to
which we removed when Hi was a few months
hold. The ’ouse in the distance is the ’ouse where
Hi spent the greater part of my heventful life.
Them ’ouses hall belongs to me. There aint hany-
think else to see from ’ere, we better go an’ ’ave a
drink.”

The Bald-Headed Metamorphosis.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I had not seen Professor Dom for months
When I entered his Sydney laboratory the otho r
day and found him neck-deep in a series of new
experiments, the nature of which were a Chines 6
puzzle to me. But the courteous old scientist sooxx
became frankly communicative.
“ What is in this gelatine, you ask 5” said lie.
“ A new idea of my own. A new benefit to the
human race, especially in the fly season. Those
are the germ-seeds of human hair. Seeds, veri-
table seeds, already spi'outing in their habitat. It
is an old joke to say that some hair-washes would
make hair grow on a billiard-ball. But the jokes
of the scoffers of one generation become the scien-
tific realities of the next. I affirm that it is not
impossible to make an ivory billiard-ball grow a
beard, any more than it is impossible to find an
Irishman with hair on his teeth. It can be done.”
“ But what is the practical utility of your in-
vention ?”
“Why, no man and no woman need be bald.
My plan is infallible. No hair-wash, no cantha-
rides, no blistering preparation, but the actual
sowing within the scalp of the hair-seeds which
you see there. It is a tickling process, but not
painful. Not half so painful as dentistry.”
“ How did you come to think of it ? ’
“Well, skin-grafting from one person to another
has long been known to surgical science; the
transfusion of blood from a healthy and vigorous
body into a debilitated one is a determined possi-
bility ; an American surgeon has successfully
transplanted live teeth from one mouth to
another ; a rabbit’s eye has been inserted to grow
in an empty socket; and it struck me that hair
might be successfully transplanted from one scalp
to another.”
“ And those haii-secds !”
“ Are all taken from the heads of hirsute and
robust people. There is male and female hair;
hair of all shades, but carefully assorted. I can
grow a crop of any desired colour.”
“ How do you sow the seed ?”
“See this scarifier? This is run over the head
of the bald individual, and the seed is then
‘creamed’in with a chemical lather of my own.
The lather is allowed to dry in till it disappears
and the whole operation is done.”
“How long does it take hair to grow?”
“It depends on the soil. Sometimes an inch
crop is produced in 24 hours ; sometimes it takes
three days. See that bristly dog; he was hairless
two day ago.”
“ Have you ever tried it on a man ?”
“Not yet. But there is a bald-headed gentle-
man coming here this morning. In fact, he is due
now. He has agreed to let mo experiment oxx his
scalp, and he has promised to pay mo handsomely
if I am successful. As I certainly shall be. Is
that the bell ? Ah, he is here. I will descend to
the door and usher him in.”
» # •
Professor Dom left the room. What
unfortunate curiosity impelled me to inspect the
gelatines microscopically ? I was very cax-eful
with them, too, but I ought to have left things
alone.
« * *
Professor Dom re-entered with a fat»
apoplectic, bald man who perspired profusely, and
breathed like a locomotive,
“This is Mr. Lumberly, Dipso., the gentlemaxx
who has agreed to try my experiment. He desires
golden hair, and I’ve no doubt he’ll be dread-
fully giddy by the time I’ve done with him. Be
seated, Mr. Lumberly.”
The Professor went straight to work. First of
all, he washed Lumberly’s head with lukewarm
water; then passed the scarifier over the scalp,
the apoplectic old gentleman the while venting
sighs like the gasps of a blowhole; next, he
“creamed” in the seeds with a yellowish lather
of a most odious odour ; then he placed the bare-
headed old gentleman in the sun to dry. The
whole opei’ation did not take longer than a-quar-
ter of an hour.
“Now, we’re right,” said the Pi'ofessor, enthu-
siastically. “As soon as the ‘creaming ’ disap-
pears you can return home, Mr. Lumberly, and
you’ll come and see me to-morrow morning.”
« * *
I stayed with Dom that night, and we
went minutely into particulars of his many inven-
tions.
Next morning a cab drove furiously up to the
door ; there was a tremendous clatter, and an
apoplectic apparition of rage burst violently into
the laboratory, without removing his hat.
“You hoary-headed, blue-spectacled, swindling
old scoundrel,” roared Lumberly, for it was he.
“You dodgasted, infernal, devilish old beast!
You elderly, unprincipled, damnable, unmitigated
skunk ! You deceiver of bald-headed patriarchs!
But you shall pay for this dearly, if there’s any
law and justice in N.S. Wales.”
“Why, wh-what’s the matter?” gasped old
Dom.
“Matter? Villainy’s the matter! Murder’s
the matter! Guy Fawkes is the matter ! I’m
the matter, you villainous old fraud ! Vermilion’s
the matter ! A rainbow’s the matter! Look at
your dastardly handiwork and dare to splutter,
‘ what’s the matter? ”
And Lmnberly removed his hat.
I have seen a few strange sights and a few blue
devils, but never such a howling polychrome as
Lumberly’s bristles. I threw myself on to a sofa
and fairly kicked with laughter. Straight out on
the lately bald man’s head stuck up a chevaux-dc-
frise of spiny hair, designed in all the tints of the
rainbow and a few more besides ! The iridescence
was the most alarmingly comical patchwork I
ever saw. It was like a speckled hen under lime-
light ; or a brindled bulldog phosphorised in the
dark.
Golden hair, brown hair, black hair, grey hair,
carroty hair, white hair, long lady’s-hair, stiff,
beardy hair—all sorts of hair standing spikily on
guard and dazzling the vision until the eyes
detected green and blue hair amongst the rain-
bows that lifted their tips angrily outwards.
With a bellow like a wounded mastiff, Lumberly
sprang at me as I lay kicking on the sofa and
pitched me clean out on to the gravelled path in
the back garden. When I came back he was
gone, and Professor Dom had gone to sleep with
his head jammed into a wedgewood mortar, while
a number of loose detonators were keeping up a
fusillade and threatening to convert the laboratory
into a flaming fireworks-show.
The action for damages, Lumberly v. Dora will
be heard at next Law Term in Sydney. Pilcher,
Salomons, Toby Barton, and Jack Want, Q.O.’s,
have all been retained by plaintiff.
“ I can’t think how the various sorts of hair-
seeds got so mixed together,” said Professor Dom;
“ I had them all sorted separately.”
I might have enlightened him, but I didn’t.
And the microscope was as mum as I was.
“But you see, the hair-sowing invention is a
dead certainty,” added Professor Horn.
“ Undoubtedly.”
THE DIPSOMANIAC.
A Break-away.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Lannigan was smart with the whip—a cleverer
manipulator of the lash never rode behind cattle.
He could cut an ordinary bottle clean in half
from the whip’s distance, and his artistic touches
were the admiration of all who knew him, and
had seen him ornament the smooth, clean bark
of a white gum with elegant scroll-work and
original designs in filigree. He boasted that he
could pick a particular hair off a cow’s ear,
riding at half-speed, or split a mosquito
on a calf’s rump over a space of 19 feet—Lannigan
used an 18-foot lash. In undertaking this latter
feat he always stipulated for a new cracker—and a
mosquito large enough to be visible at the distance.
As an implement of trade, too, Tom got all the
business out of his whip that could be reasonably
expected from a lash in mere human hands. He
could bring tour feet of his “teaser” across the
bows of a break-away, and make the “fur” fly all
along the line, or touch him on the raw with
the knot, and sting like a bullet ; but the
trick of which he was proudest, and which proved
the most useful to him as a drovei’, was the knack
of curling the lash about the body of a “bolting”
calf, hoisting the animal, and bringing it up short,
and back to reason and the drove, without further
waste of horse:flesh, time, or profanity. Calves,
when the mischief is in them, are hard to engineer,
as any drover will tell you, and this trick of trade
was worth a heap to Lannigan.
Tom was a young man, and excepting a weak-
ness for drinks, another for the girls, and a faculty
for bad language, original and selected, he was as
good as the best. One sale day he was bringing a
mob away from the Ballarat yards. The cattle
were all fat and respectable, excepting one large,
red brute with a blazed face and a touch of the
furies. This animal had knocked himself about a
good deal in the yards, one horn was broken off
short and dangled against his jaw 7, and the thick
blood coated his head ; a dark foam hung about
his mouth, and his red eyes sparkled hatefully in
the sunlight. The big bullock refused to chum in
with the mob, and moaned ominously as he ran
ahead swinging from side to side and looking
fairly demented.
Lannigan expected trouble with this brute, and
was not kept waiting. The beast broke away pre-
sently, and made across the flat towards the
timber. Tom rode a rnare new to the business ;
she had too much “go ” as yet, and over-stepped
her game. Lannigan handled her well, how-
ever, and soon had the whip going on
the run-away, doing his best to head him.
The race was at its hottest when the
sound of a woman’s screams attracted Tom’s
attention. He looked up, and the first thing to
catch his eye was the figure of a little child directly
ahead at some distance. The youngster wore a
bright hood and toddled towards them, regardless
of the cries of a woman who, recognising the
danger, was rushing to save her child. Tom’s
eyes fell upon the red bullock again, and he
noticed the lowered head and the determined set
of the animal, and guessed its purpose. Swinging
the. whip again he worked it about the head of the
brute with all his power, bringing the mare up to
close quarters at the same time, and boring reek-
lessly. It was useless ;he recognised this in a few
seconds, and then he received an inspiration.
Driving the spurs into his nag, lie rode for the
child. There was a thunder of hoofs, a wild
whoop from Lannigan, as he rose in his stirrups,
and brought his long lash down on the youngster
with a circular movement, and in the wink of an
eye the little girl with the bright pink hood was
safe and sound in the drover’s arms; and the big
in of hie

Dear Bulletin,—ln the palmy days of the
Etheridge (N.Q.), three brothers worked a claim
known as the “Better Luck.” One of them
Paddy, on one occasion was “ on a tearing spree ’’’
Father Maginnis, a muscular clergyman, hap-
pened, at the time, to be on a visit to George-
town, and persuaded Pat to take the pledge ; lest
temptation should prove too much for the novice,
whilst suffering a recovery, the reverend gentlc-
man securely strapped Patrick down on his bag-
stretcher. The good priest then retired to bis
hotel, and was engaged in prayer, as became his
vocation, when he was startled by a most infernal
clatter along the boarded verandah, and on look-
iDg for the cause discovered the decent boy from
the “ Better Luck ” trying to force his vfry into
the bar, stretcher and all.—Yours, Aramac.

The last nigger-execution in U csfc Aus-
tralia is reported as a great success. There were
three coloured gentlemen to be_ asphyxiated at
Mount Dockrell—an outlying region where there
is no gallows or other evidence of civilisation,
So a deep pit was dug, and a tip-dray drawn up
along side of it. Then the three little niggers
were seated all in a row on the edge of the dray,
with their arras tied, and their feet hanging over
the pit, and each man had a rope hitched round
his neck, the other end of the rope being secured
to a gum-tree overhead. Last of all, the hang-
man gave a great heave that sent the shafts of the
dray up in*the air, and the fated three slid off
with a howl and a screech into the excavation,
an .d two of them hung there in peace, while the
third bounced up again like an india-rubber devil
jumping out of a box, and showed a blue
countenance and a bristling shock of hair, and two
6 V es Biat stuck out like bell-toppers on a hat-rack,
f° r one instant above the edge of the pit. Then
he fell back like the,rest, and the ceremony was
over.

Delusion.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Last night I had a happy dream:
Methought I called your name—
Beside the lonely winding stream
I called you, and you came.
For as I roamed, through mist and rain
The past my memory drew,
Till, fraught with passion and with pain,
My heart cried out to you.
I know not how you heard me call,
For we are far apart;
The lonely bush holds me in thrall,
And you the city’s heart.
But after years of silence sore,
And loneliness and pain,
You held me in your arms once more,
You kissed me once again.
We cared not for the waning light,
Nor heard the plover’s cry,
As, overhead, he wheeled his flight
Beneath the wintry sky.
For, weeping in your arms at last
I told the tale of years,
And all the sorrows of the past
Were swept away in tears.
The yellow willow-branches swayed.
There fell a golden shower;
It seemed that all my griefs were paid
In that one blissful hour !
And then you swore a mighty oath—
You held me fast and swore —
That nought but death should part us both,
Should part us evermore.
• ••••»
I woke. Myself alone I found,
And filled with strange alarms ;
The cruel darkness closed me round,
And not your brave, strong arms.
The tears were still upon my check,
But all the rest was fled—
The lonely plain, the winding creek.
The willows overhead.
I only dreamed the rain and mist,
The plover and the stream !
My love ! my love ! Say, have you kissed
Me only in a dream ?
And we arc still apart, my love !
You think me still untrue.
lam not so ! As God’s above
I never loved but you.
Alas 1 he hears not what I say !
What need for me to swear ?
The fond delusion melts away,
And we are as we were.
And thronging backwards, thick and fast,
Come all my griefs and fears.
The dream, the happiness, has passed
And only left the tears !
N. S. Wales.
CLAUDIA.
Rev. Dr. Bevan, the most comfortable,
cushiony-lookin'? parson in all Australia, tells a
Melbourne audience that he rejoces over the
great depression, which was needed to check the
people’s vices—gambling and extravagance. The
round and rosy divine draws <£33 a week for in-
culcating frugality and abstemiousness in people
who consider themselves lucky when- they earn
30s. for fifty hours’ work.
Wolfe's Schnapps is the only pure and original
Schnapps. To be safe, ask for an unopened bottle.
When the pious proprietors of Melbourne
Daily Telegraph decided to stop the ruinous
publication of that respectable journal, their chief
reason, as alleged in their new mouthpiece, the
Evening ’Euald, was that the wicked composi-
tors had refused to accept a lower rate of pay
than that given by the Argus and the Age.
“ They were tired of carrying on their busi-
ness for the benefit of their workmen,” they
said. But as their losses were about £1 50
per week, it is not easy to see how a body
of forty-five compositors could have made up the
money by consenting to a reduction of a penny a
thousand. There are 30,000 pence in .£l5O, and
the compositors would have had to set 30,000,000
letters a week before they could satisfy their
employers. This would have brought the cost of
composition up to £2lOO per week, the truth
being that it was not one-tenth of that sum. The
real reason of the downfall of the journal, which
paid fairly well a dozen years ago under Sornerton
and Willoughby, was that when Balfour and
Davies and Fitchett and M‘Kinley got hold of it
those godly men gave it a fit of the snuffles from
which it never recovered.
. Fifty years ago, Thomas Cooper, the
Chartist poet, was prosecuted for sedition and
thrust into a British dungeon as a rebel. Twenty
years earlier he would have been hanged and
beheaded, like the two democrats at Stirling, but
in 1842 a term of penal servitude was considered
sufficient punishment for the miscreant who wrote
“ Slaves, toil no more ! Why delve and moil and
Pine to glut the tyrant-forgers of your chain ”
Thomas Cooper didn’t go the way of most hard
thinkers fated to be born before the world is ready
for them, and die at an early ago of sheer disgust
Hu constitution was proof against even a course
of prison treatment in the “forties.” And so it
came to pass a few weeks since that the First
Lord of the Treasury granted £230 to Thomas
Cooper, now in his eighty-eighth year, “in recog-
nition of Mr. Cooper’s literary talent and influence
an a moral teacher:'’ The old Chartist has lived to
receive a small token of respect from the same
State which formerly saw immorality, pestilence
and flat political blasphemy in his “moral
teaching.” To the dangerous socialist of 1892
The BULLETIN says : Look after your liver, avoid
draughts and take precautions against the in-
fluenza, that your days may be long in the land
where the wild “fallacies” of to-day are the
sober truths of to-morrow. Don’t get run over by
a tram, or die with all your advanced errors on
your head but hang on to existence until the main
army of plodding fools has overtaken your ideas
and passed a grant of .£2OO to keep your recog-
nised worth out of the alms-house.
Pfahlert’s Hotel, Wynyard Square, Sydney
J. Lichtscheindl, for many years proprietor of the Empire
Hotel, Wellington, N.Z., has taken over the above old
established first-class hotel. The Garden Restaurant is
now ready for the summer months.
The increase in the size of English youth
is scientifically ascribed to the abolition of phle-
botomy m the generation before last.
Wain’s Hotel, Dunedin (M.L.), has been
refused a license. Why, is a mystery up to date,
for it was one of the very best hostelries in Aus-
tralasia, and a few years ago was adorned by the
prettiest woman who ever agitated a beer-pump.
Also, there was a convenient back-door, whereby
you could get out of the hotel into one Benjamin’s
place in Manse-street, and discount a bill without
attracting attention.
i Wages are coming down fast in Melbourne.
The contractors for cleaning the city streets, who
took the job very much below the previous rate,
have knocked a big percentage off the wages of
their employes after drawing their attention to the
fact that thousands of men are walking about,
who would be glad of their jobs. This is one of
the beauties of the contract system. Governments
and corporations get their work done cheaply at
the cost of the labourer. Why not have the clerical
work dona by contract?
D Arcy Brothers, Brokers, Scott Chambers
90 i itt-street, Sydney,
Recently a Launceston (Tas.) jury struck,
after four hours on the job, and demanded lunch, as
they could get no further without something to
eat. Many a man has feed a lawyer who would
have fared much bettor could he have fed the jury.
In Pennsylvania, the Hungarians and
Croats, who build railroads and work in the
mines, have established a system of polyandry,
under which each woman has six or eight hus-
bands. And in this State there are some fifty
thousand Chinamen with not much more than
two or three thousand women among them.
It is like a voice from the grave to read
Thomas Smith Richardson’s advertisement as it
looms up to this day on the back of the Balmain
Ferry Co.’s tickets, setting forth that his dead
and execrated bank accepts deposits at the highest
rates of interest, and offers undoubted security to
the investor. And we came across another finan-
cial ghost the other day in the British Austra-
lasian, a London paper, which regularly every
week quotes the Bank of Van Diemen’s Land in
its share-list as a going concern, that pays divi-
dends, and has a big reserve-fund, and whose
shares are traded in on the Stock Exchange.

manager’s man.
Dear Bulletin,---Re poor Barcroft Boake’s
verses “Featherstonhaugh,” printed by you h,
other day. The real name of Bluecap, the oiif
law, was Bobby Cottrell, and he was employed
a boy on Benduck station. In those days
police, when hunting bushrangers, could by ] :u !j
demand remounts from anyone possessing horses
One old sergeant in particular was very fond of
this style of thing, and very often forgot to return
the nags. One day the owner was going awav
from the station, and, knowing that the police
were likely to call in his absence, he told Bobbv
not to let them have horses if it could possibly bo
avoided. When the boss had gone Bobby planted
all the horses except one-a hopeless rogue, that
would buck its saddle off if mounted by a stranger
Presently the sergeant called and demanded
horses, so Bobby brought out the bucker, which
at once threw the officer. Nor could any others of
the police party ride him. Bobby stood bv
laughing at the troopers’ discomfiture, and, s ' o
goes the legend, the sergeant swore vengeance
and from that day forward tried all he could to
lag Robert, who in the end, under the name of
“Bluecap,” took to the bush with a mate called
“Jack the Devil.” What became of Jack, I don’t
know, but Bobby was captured and got 15 years
in Cockatoo.—The Dingo. Blackheath, ’ 12tl,
